جواب :۔ دیا کہ آپ کی بات درست ہے کہ قطعی حکم اس وقت ہو گا جب ثبوت قطعی ہو ہماری مراد بھی یہی ہے کیونکہ نص اور اجتماع یقین کا فائدہ دیتے ہیں الا یہ کہ کوئی عارض پایا جائے تو پھر یہ قطعیت کا فائدہ نہیں دیتے لیکن ہماری بحث اس میں چل رہی ہے کہ نص اور اجماع قطع نظر عوارض کے یہ مفید للقطع ہیں لہذا جب ذات کے اعتبار سے مفید للقطع ہیں تو فقہ میں داخل رہیں گے فقہ سے خارج نہیں ہونگے ۔

مات کے جواب ثالث کی توضیح

قولہ و ثالثاً : یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے اس میں شارع نے متن میں مذکورہ اعتراض کے تیسرے جواب کی وضاحت کی ہے۔ تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ ایک ہے مسائل قیاسیہ تک پہنچنے کا طریق اور دوسرا ہے خود مسائل قیاسیہ تو مسائل قیاسیہ میں قطن نہیں ہے البتہ خطن طریق میں ہے یعنی معلوم نہیں کہ مجتہد مطلوب تک پہنچے گا یا نہیں جب پہنچ گیا تو ظن نہیں رہا یعنی جب حکم ثابت ہو گیا مجتہد کے نزدیک اور مسائل قیاسیہ کا استنباط کر لیا ہے نصوص سے تو ظن نہیں رہا۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ اگر مسائل قیاسیہ فقہ میں داخل ہوں پھر بھی فقہ قطعی رہے گا اس لئے اس پر علم کا اطلاق درست ہے۔

تقریرہ:۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے اس میں شاریخ نے مذکورہ جواب کی تفصیل کی ہے قبل از تفصیل ایک مقدم تمہید کے طور پر سمجھیں کہ علماء کے دو طبقے ہیں (۱) ایک طبقہ یہ کہتا ہے کہ ہر مجتہد مصیب ہے اور ہر مجتہد کا اجتہاد درست ہے (۲) دوسرا طبقہ یہ کہتا ہے کہ ایک مجتہد مصیب ہے اور درست ہے باقی کو غلطی لاحق ہو سکتی ہے پہلے طبقے کو مصوبہ اور دوسرے کو غیر مصوبہ کہتے ہیں اب تمہید کے بعد تفصیل سمجھیں کہ جب اجماع سے ثابت ہے کہ ظن غالب موجب الیقین ہے یعنی کسی کام لليقين میں ظن غالب ہو جائے تو یقین کا فائدہ ہوگا اور اس پر بہت ساری اخبار احاد دلالت کرتی ہیں کہ ظن غالب موجب سیلین ہے اور یہ روایات الفاظ کے اعتبار سے اگر چہ احاد ہیں معنی مفہوم کے اعتبار سے تواتر کی حد کو پہنچی ہوئی ہیں کہ ظن غالب موجب الیقین ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ شریعت نے ظن غالب کا اعتبار کر لیا ہے اب شریعہ کی طرف سے گویا کہ نص ہو گئی کہ ہر وہ حکم جس میں مجتہد کو طن غالب حاصل ہو وہ اللہ کے علم میں ثابت ہے اور مسائل قیاسیہ جتنے ہیں ان میں مجتہد کا ظن غالب پایا جاتا ہے تو یہ اللہ کے علم میں ثابت ہیں فلہذا یہ قطعی ہوئے یہ فرقہ مصوبہ کے نزدیک ہے اور جنہوں نے کہا ۔ ہر مجتہد مصیب نہیں ہے دویوں کہتے ہیں کہ اجماع میں ظن غالب اور روایات میں ظن غالب کا اعتبار ہے تو یوں استدلال کرتے ہیں کلما غلب علیہ ظن المجعد فقد
That which does not have the possibility of non-existence in reality, and the utmost effort possible at this point is that which some investigators have mentioned in the commentary of Minhaj, and it is that indeed the ruling that is conjectural (maznun) for the mujtahid is obligatory for him to act upon it definitively due to a definitive proof. And every ruling that it is obligatory to act upon will certainly be known to be Allah's ruling; otherwise, acting upon it would not be obligatory. And every ruling that is known definitively to be the ruling of Allah, then it is known definitively. So every ruling that is obligatory to act upon definitively is known definitively. Thus the ruling that is conjectural for the mujtahid is known definitively. So fiqh is definitive knowledge, and conjecture is a means to it. And its solution is that indeed we do not accept that indeed every such ruling that it is obligatory to act upon definitively is known definitively to be Allah's ruling. Why is it not permissible that acting definitively be obligatory concerning something that is conjectured to be Allah's ruling? So his statement, 'otherwise acting upon it would not be obligatory,' is the very point of dispute. And if he bases it on 'everything conjectured by the mujtahid is definitely the ruling of Allah' as is the opinion of some, then mentioning the obligation of acting would be futile; no meaning would remain at all.

The commentator's response to the first answer of the matn

Elaboration: His statement 'so his first answer': In this phrase, the commentator made two objections. Earlier, the matn had answered the objection that fiqh is conjectural and knowledge is not applied to conjectures, so fiqh should not be called 'ilm al-fiqh. The matn answered that fiqh is the name of those rulings that appeared from revelation or upon which consensus was formed, and these are definitive, so calling it 'ilm al-fiqh is correct. Now the commentator made two objections. Objection (1): This would necessitate that qiyasi issues are excluded from fiqh, whereas they are among the major issues of fiqh. Answer: This is that qiyasi issues are included in compiled fiqh (fiqh mudawwan), not in real fiqh (fiqh haqiqi). This answer has already passed earlier, so the objection no longer remains. Objection (2): It is as if you said that those rulings upon which consensus has been formed or revelation has appeared are certainly definitive. We do not accept their definitiveness because they can only be definitive when their proof (thubut) is also definitive. Because if a ruling is proven by a solitary report (khabar wahid), this proof is not definitive but is called conjectural. Similarly, rulings proven by consensus are not considered definitive unless the consensus itself is proven definitively, i.e., consensus is proven by mutawatir or mashhur report. For example, if one person reports the consensus, the proof is not definitive, and thus the ruling is also not definitive.

Answer: Granted that your statement is correct: that a definitive ruling occurs only when its proof is definitive. That is also our intention. Because text (nass) and consensus (ijma) give benefit of certainty unless some obstacle ('arid) is found; then they do not give the benefit of definitiveness. But our discussion is that text and consensus, regardless of obstacles, are beneficial for definitiveness. Therefore, since they are beneficial for definitiveness by their essence, they will remain included in fiqh and will not be excluded from fiqh.

Clarification of the third answer of the matn

His statement 'and thirdly': This is the first part of the commentary's phrase. In it, the commentator clarifies the third answer to the mentioned objection in the text. Its detail is that there is one thing: the method of reaching qiyasi issues, and another thing: the qiyasi issues themselves. There is no conjecture (zann) in the qiyasi issues themselves, but there is conjecture in the method. That is, it is not known whether the mujtahid will reach the sought ruling or not. When he reaches it, conjecture no longer remains; i.e., when the ruling is established according to the mujtahid and he has derived the qiyasi issues from the texts, then conjecture is gone. Summary: Even if qiyasi issues are included in fiqh, still fiqh remains definitive. Therefore, applying 'knowledge' to it is correct.

Taqrir: This is the second part of the commentary's phrase. In it, the commentator details the mentioned answer. Before the detail, understand as a preamble that scholars are of two groups: (1) One group says that every mujtahid is correct (musib) and the ijtihad of every mujtahid is valid. (2) The second group says that only one mujtahid is correct and valid; the rest may err. The first group is called Musawwiba, the second Ghayr Musawwiba. Now after the preamble, understand the detail: Since it is established by consensus that preponderant conjecture (zann ghalib) is equivalent to certainty (yaqin), i.e., in a matter that requires certainty, preponderant conjecture will give the benefit of certainty. Many solitary reports (akhbar ahad) indicate that preponderant conjecture is equivalent to certainty. Although these reports are solitary in wording, in terms of meaning and concept they have reached the level of tawatur that preponderant conjecture is equivalent to certainty. So it is known that Shari'ah has accepted preponderant conjecture. Now from the side of Shari'ah, it is as if there is a text that every ruling in which the mujtahid obtains preponderant conjecture is established in Allah's knowledge. And all qiyasi issues contain the mujtahid's preponderant conjecture, so they are established in Allah's knowledge. Hence they became definitive. This is according to the Musawwiba group. As for those who said that not every mujtahid is correct, the others say that in consensus and in narrations, preponderant conjecture is accepted. They thus argue: 'Everything that is preponderantly conjectured by the mujtahid...'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) on Al-Tawdih, addressing objections to the claim that fiqh is definitive knowledge. The passage has two main sections: response to the first objection and clarification of the third response. In the first section, the commentator raises two objections against the matn's answer that fiqh is qati' because it is based on revelation and ijma. Objection 1: This excludes qiyasi issues from fiqh. The reply: qiyasi issues are part of compiled fiqh (fiqh mudawwan) not real fiqh (fiqh haqiqi). Objection 2: The proof (thubut) of these rulings must also be qati' to make the ruling qati'. The reply: text (nass) and consensus (ijma) are inherently beneficial for certainty, barring any obstacle. So the essence is qati'. In the second section, the third response is clarified: the method of deriving qiyasi issues involves conjecture, but once the mujtahid derives them, they become certain for him. The author then discusses the Musawwiba vs Ghayr Musawwiba debate: according to Musawwiba, zann ghalib is equivalent to certainty based on ijma and ahadith, making qiyasi issues qati'. For Ghayr Musawwiba, zann ghalib is still accepted as authoritative. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to the certainty of fiqh despite the use of conjecture in ijtihad.

Key Terms
فقہ حقیقی (fiqh haqiqi): Real fiqh: the rulings themselves that are directly from nas (Quran/Sunnah) or ijma, which are definitively known.
فقہ مدون (fiqh mudawwan): Compiled fiqh: the collected rulings including those derived by qiyas and ijtihad, which form the written works of the school.
مسائل قیاسیہ (masa'il qiyasiyya): Qiyasi issues: rulings derived through analogical reasoning (qiyas) from primary sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of qiyasi issues in relation to fiqh?
A) They are excluded from fiqh entirely.
B) They are part of fiqh haqiqi.
C) They are part of fiqh mudawwan.
D) They are not considered knowledge at all.

Key Takeaway
Qiyasi issues are included in compiled fiqh but not in real fiqh, preserving the definitive nature of fiqh's core.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Certainty of Fiqh: The Objection from Thubut and the Response
Response to second objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers rulings from nas and ijma as definitively certain (qat'i) even when their proof is zanni, by invoking the inherent beneficial quality of these sources.

Core Idea
Nas and ijma are inherently beneficial for certainty; obstacles do not negate their essential qat'i nature.

The second objection challenges the definitiveness of rulings from nas and ijma unless their proof (thubut) is also definitive. The Hanafi response argues that nas and ijma are inherently beneficial for certainty by their essence, regardless of how they are transmitted. This lesson unpacks that response and its implications for the epistemology of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Restate the objection
The objector says: For a ruling to be qat'i, its proof must also be qat'i. If the ruling is from khabar wahid or ijma reported by one person, the thubut is zanni, so the ruling cannot be qat'i.
This objection attacks the second pillar: not the source of the ruling, but its transmission.
2
Understand the Hanafi concession
The commentator agrees: a ruling is qat'i only if its thubut is qat'i. That is also his intention.
This shows the Hanafi school does not ignore the need for transmission certainty.
3
Identify the key move
The critical point: when we add the qualification 'barring obstacles' (عوارض), the ruling can still be considered qat'i in essence. Because nas and ijma are inherently beneficial for certainty (ذاتاً مفید للقطع).
Obstacles refer to situations where there is a known flaw or conflict, not to mere possibility of error.
4
Apply to fiqh
Thus, rulings from nas and ijma, considered in themselves (without obstacles), are qat'i. They remain included in fiqh because the discipline studies them in their essential state.
This preserves the definitive nature of fiqh even if some individual rulings have zanni thubut in practice.
5
Conclude
The objection fails to recognize that the essential beneficial property of nas and ijma grants certainty, and the discussion in usul is about that essence.
This sets up the third response regarding zann ghalib.

مات کے جواب اول پر شاریخ کارد

تشریح: قولہ فجوابہ اولا:۔ اس عبارت میں شارع نے دو اعتراض کئے ہیں ماقبل میں ماتن نے اعتراض کا جواب دیا تھا اعتراض یہ تھا ماتن پر کہ فقہ ظنی ہے اور خلیات پر علم کا اطلاق نہیں ہوتا تو فقہ کو علم الفقہ نہیں کہنا چاہیے تو ماتن نے جواب دیا تھا کہ فقہ نام ہے ان احکام کا جن کا ظہور نزول وحی سے ہوا ہو یا ان پر اجماع منعقد ہوا ہو اور یہ قطعی ہیں تو علم الفقہ کہنا صحیح ہے اب شاریخ نے دو اعتراض کئے ہیں۔ اعتراض (۱)۔ اس سے لازم آئے گا کہ مسائل قیاسیہ فقہ سے خارج ہوں حالانکہ یہ فقہ کے معظم مسائل میں سے ہیں جواب :۔ یہ ہے کہ مسائل قیاسیہ فقہ مدون میں داخل ہیں نہ کہ فقہ حقیقی میں یہ جواب ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے لہذا اب اعتراض نہ رہا۔ اعتراض (۲) ۔ گویا کہ آپ نے کہا کہ جن احکام پر اجماع کا انعقاد ہوا ہو یا ان پر نزول وحی کا ظہور ہوا ہو یہ یقینا قطعی ہیں ہم ان کا قطعی ہونا تسلیم نہیں کرتے کیونکہ وہ قطعی تب ہو سکتے ہیں جب ان کا ثبوت بھی قطعی ہو اس لئے کہ اگر حکم خبر واحد سے ثابت ہو تو یہ ثبوت قطعی نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کو ظنی کہتے ہیں ایسے ہی اجماع سے جو احکام ثابت ہوں یہ بھی قطعی ہوں یہ نہیں مانتے

جب تک اجماع کا ثبوت قطعی نہ ہو یعنی اجماع خبر متواتر یاخبر مشہور سے ثابت ہو چنانچہ اگر کسی ایک آدمی نے اجماع کی خبر دے دی تو ثبوت قطعی نہیں ہو گا جب ثبوت قطعی نہیں ہوگا تو حکم بھی قطعی نہیں ہوگا۔

جواب :۔ دیا کہ آپ کی بات درست ہے کہ قطعی حکم اس وقت ہو گا جب ثبوت قطعی ہو ہماری مراد بھی یہی ہے کیونکہ نص اور اجتماع یقین کا فائدہ دیتے ہیں الا یہ کہ کوئی عارض پایا جائے تو پھر یہ قطعیت کا فائدہ نہیں دیتے لیکن ہماری بحث اس میں چل رہی ہے کہ نص اور اجماع قطع نظر عوارض کے یہ مفید للقطع ہیں لہذا جب ذات کے اعتبار سے مفید للقطع ہیں تو فقہ میں داخل رہیں گے فقہ سے خارج نہیں ہونگے ۔

مات کے جواب ثالث کی توضیح

قولہ و ثالثاً : یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے اس میں شارع نے متن میں مذکورہ اعتراض کے تیسرے جواب کی وضاحت کی ہے۔ تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ ایک ہے مسائل قیاسیہ تک پہنچنے کا طریق اور دوسرا ہے خود مسائل قیاسیہ تو مسائل قیاسیہ میں قطن نہیں ہے البتہ خطن طریق میں ہے یعنی معلوم نہیں کہ مجتہد مطلوب تک پہنچے گا یا نہیں جب پہنچ گیا تو ظن نہیں رہا یعنی جب حکم ثابت ہو گیا مجتہد کے نزدیک اور مسائل قیاسیہ کا استنباط کر لیا ہے نصوص سے تو ظن نہیں رہا۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ اگر مسائل قیاسیہ فقہ میں داخل ہوں پھر بھی فقہ قطعی رہے گا اس لئے اس پر علم کا اطلاق درست ہے۔

تقریرہ:۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے اس میں شاریخ نے مذکورہ جواب کی تفصیل کی ہے قبل از تفصیل ایک مقدم تمہید کے طور پر سمجھیں کہ علماء کے دو طبقے ہیں (۱) ایک طبقہ یہ کہتا ہے کہ ہر مجتہد مصیب ہے اور ہر مجتہد کا اجتہاد درست ہے (۲) دوسرا طبقہ یہ کہتا ہے کہ ایک مجتہد مصیب ہے اور درست ہے باقی کو غلطی لاحق ہو سکتی ہے پہلے طبقے کو مصوبہ اور دوسرے کو غیر مصوبہ کہتے ہیں اب تمہید کے بعد تفصیل سمجھیں کہ جب اجماع سے ثابت ہے کہ ظن غالب موجب الیقین ہے یعنی کسی کام لليقين میں ظن غالب ہو جائے تو یقین کا فائدہ ہوگا اور اس پر بہت ساری اخبار احاد دلالت کرتی ہیں کہ ظن غالب موجب سیلین ہے اور یہ روایات الفاظ کے اعتبار سے اگر چہ احاد ہیں معنی مفہوم کے اعتبار سے تواتر کی حد کو پہنچی ہوئی ہیں کہ ظن غالب موجب الیقین ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ شریعت نے ظن غالب کا اعتبار کر لیا ہے اب شریعہ کی طرف سے گویا کہ نص ہو گئی کہ ہر وہ حکم جس میں مجتہد کو طن غالب حاصل ہو وہ اللہ کے علم میں ثابت ہے اور مسائل قیاسیہ جتنے ہیں ان میں مجتہد کا ظن غالب پایا جاتا ہے تو یہ اللہ کے علم میں ثابت ہیں فلہذا یہ قطعی ہوئے یہ فرقہ مصوبہ کے نزدیک ہے اور جنہوں نے کہا ۔ ہر مجتہد مصیب نہیں ہے دویوں کہتے ہیں کہ اجماع میں ظن غالب اور روایات میں ظن غالب کا اعتبار ہے تو یوں استدلال کرتے ہیں کلما غلب علیہ ظن المجعد فقد
That which does not have the possibility of non-existence in reality, and the utmost effort possible at this point is that which some investigators have mentioned in the commentary of Minhaj, and it is that indeed the ruling that is conjectural (maznun) for the mujtahid is obligatory for him to act upon it definitively due to a definitive proof. And every ruling that it is obligatory to act upon will certainly be known to be Allah's ruling; otherwise, acting upon it would not be obligatory. And every ruling that is known definitively to be the ruling of Allah, then it is known definitively. So every ruling that is obligatory to act upon definitively is known definitively. Thus the ruling that is conjectural for the mujtahid is known definitively. So fiqh is definitive knowledge, and conjecture is a means to it. And its solution is that indeed we do not accept that indeed every such ruling that it is obligatory to act upon definitively is known definitively to be Allah's ruling. Why is it not permissible that acting definitively be obligatory concerning something that is conjectured to be Allah's ruling? So his statement, 'otherwise acting upon it would not be obligatory,' is the very point of dispute. And if he bases it on 'everything conjectured by the mujtahid is definitely the ruling of Allah' as is the opinion of some, then mentioning the obligation of acting would be futile; no meaning would remain at all.

The commentator's response to the first answer of the matn

Elaboration: His statement 'so his first answer': In this phrase, the commentator made two objections. Earlier, the matn had answered the objection that fiqh is conjectural and knowledge is not applied to conjectures, so fiqh should not be called 'ilm al-fiqh. The matn answered that fiqh is the name of those rulings that appeared from revelation or upon which consensus was formed, and these are definitive, so calling it 'ilm al-fiqh is correct. Now the commentator made two objections. Objection (1): This would necessitate that qiyasi issues are excluded from fiqh, whereas they are among the major issues of fiqh. Answer: This is that qiyasi issues are included in compiled fiqh (fiqh mudawwan), not in real fiqh (fiqh haqiqi). This answer has already passed earlier, so the objection no longer remains. Objection (2): It is as if you said that those rulings upon which consensus has been formed or revelation has appeared are certainly definitive. We do not accept their definitiveness because they can only be definitive when their proof (thubut) is also definitive. Because if a ruling is proven by a solitary report (khabar wahid), this proof is not definitive but is called conjectural. Similarly, rulings proven by consensus are not considered definitive unless the consensus itself is proven definitively, i.e., consensus is proven by mutawatir or mashhur report. For example, if one person reports the consensus, the proof is not definitive, and thus the ruling is also not definitive.

Answer: Granted that your statement is correct: that a definitive ruling occurs only when its proof is definitive. That is also our intention. Because text (nass) and consensus (ijma) give benefit of certainty unless some obstacle ('arid) is found; then they do not give the benefit of definitiveness. But our discussion is that text and consensus, regardless of obstacles, are beneficial for definitiveness. Therefore, since they are beneficial for definitiveness by their essence, they will remain included in fiqh and will not be excluded from fiqh.

Clarification of the third answer of the matn

His statement 'and thirdly': This is the first part of the commentary's phrase. In it, the commentator clarifies the third answer to the mentioned objection in the text. Its detail is that there is one thing: the method of reaching qiyasi issues, and another thing: the qiyasi issues themselves. There is no conjecture (zann) in the qiyasi issues themselves, but there is conjecture in the method. That is, it is not known whether the mujtahid will reach the sought ruling or not. When he reaches it, conjecture no longer remains; i.e., when the ruling is established according to the mujtahid and he has derived the qiyasi issues from the texts, then conjecture is gone. Summary: Even if qiyasi issues are included in fiqh, still fiqh remains definitive. Therefore, applying 'knowledge' to it is correct.

Taqrir: This is the second part of the commentary's phrase. In it, the commentator details the mentioned answer. Before the detail, understand as a preamble that scholars are of two groups: (1) One group says that every mujtahid is correct (musib) and the ijtihad of every mujtahid is valid. (2) The second group says that only one mujtahid is correct and valid; the rest may err. The first group is called Musawwiba, the second Ghayr Musawwiba. Now after the preamble, understand the detail: Since it is established by consensus that preponderant conjecture (zann ghalib) is equivalent to certainty (yaqin), i.e., in a matter that requires certainty, preponderant conjecture will give the benefit of certainty. Many solitary reports (akhbar ahad) indicate that preponderant conjecture is equivalent to certainty. Although these reports are solitary in wording, in terms of meaning and concept they have reached the level of tawatur that preponderant conjecture is equivalent to certainty. So it is known that Shari'ah has accepted preponderant conjecture. Now from the side of Shari'ah, it is as if there is a text that every ruling in which the mujtahid obtains preponderant conjecture is established in Allah's knowledge. And all qiyasi issues contain the mujtahid's preponderant conjecture, so they are established in Allah's knowledge. Hence they became definitive. This is according to the Musawwiba group. As for those who said that not every mujtahid is correct, the others say that in consensus and in narrations, preponderant conjecture is accepted. They thus argue: 'Everything that is preponderantly conjectured by the mujtahid...'
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) on Al-Tawdih, addressing objections to the claim that fiqh is definitive knowledge. The passage has two main sections: response to the first objection and clarification of the third response. In the first section, the commentator raises two objections against the matn's answer that fiqh is qati' because it is based on revelation and ijma. Objection 1: This excludes qiyasi issues from fiqh. The reply: qiyasi issues are part of compiled fiqh (fiqh mudawwan) not real fiqh (fiqh haqiqi). Objection 2: The proof (thubut) of these rulings must also be qati' to make the ruling qati'. The reply: text (nass) and consensus (ijma) are inherently beneficial for certainty, barring any obstacle. So the essence is qati'. In the second section, the third response is clarified: the method of deriving qiyasi issues involves conjecture, but once the mujtahid derives them, they become certain for him. The author then discusses the Musawwiba vs Ghayr Musawwiba debate: according to Musawwiba, zann ghalib is equivalent to certainty based on ijma and ahadith, making qiyasi issues qati'. For Ghayr Musawwiba, zann ghalib is still accepted as authoritative. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to the certainty of fiqh despite the use of conjecture in ijtihad.

Key Terms
ذاتاً مفيد للقطع (dhatan mufid lil-qat'): Inherently beneficial for certainty: the quality of nas and ijma to produce definitive knowledge by their very nature, regardless of external factors.
عارض ('arid): Obstacle or accidental factor that prevents a source from yielding certainty, such as a known contradiction or a flaw in the transmission.
ثبوت (thubut): The establishment or proof of a report's transmission; its authenticity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are rulings from nas and ijma considered qat'i even if their thubut is zanni?
A) Because the Hanafi school accepts that any conjecture is certainty.
B) Because they are inherently beneficial for certainty, barring obstacles.
C) Because the scholars have agreed to ignore obstacles.
D) Because qiyas converts them to certainty.

Key Takeaway
Nas and ijma are inherently beneficial for certainty; obstacles may prevent the benefit but do not change their essential nature.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
12 min
The Certainty of Fiqh: Defending Qiyasi Rulings as Decisive
Defending the certainty of qiyasi rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that fiqh rulings become certain (qat'i) after ijtihad and reconstruct the two-syllogism proof.

Core Idea
After ijtihad, predominant conjecture becomes certain in obligation, making fiqh a decisive science.

This lesson tackles a critical objection in Hanafi usul: if fiqh rulings are derived through speculative reasoning (zann), how can they be called certain knowledge ('ilm)? The commentator on al-Tawdih provides a sophisticated response, distinguishing between the time before and after ijtihad. You will follow the logical structure of the Hanafi defense and see how consensus (ijma) and ahad hadith serve as evidence that the conjectured becomes decisive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the first block of the source where the objector argues: if qiyasi rulings are speculative (zanni), they cannot be decisive (qat'i) because the decisive admits no opposite possibility. Note the apparent contradiction.
The objector assumes that a ruling must be either speculative or decisive at all times.
2
Distinguish the Two Stages
The commentator answers by separating the pre-ijtihad stage (the ruling is speculative) from the post-ijtihad stage (the ruling becomes decisive for the mujtahid). Understand that the contradiction is resolved because the two times differ.
This is a key Hanafi move: certainty is subjective to the mujtahid's ijtihad, not objective in nafs al-amr.
3
Analyze the First Syllogism
The commentator presents a formal syllogism: (Minor) Every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid is obligatorily acted upon decisively. (Major) Everything obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively to be Allah's ruling. The evidence for the minor is ijma and ahad hadith with cumulative meaning (mutawatir bi al-ma'na).
The minor premise connects zann ghalib to decisive obligation; the major connects obligation to divine ruling.
4
Analyze the Second Syllogism and Combination
The second syllogism shows that what is obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively to be Allah's ruling. Combining with the first yields: every conjectured ruling is known decisively. Thus fiqh (which comprises such rulings) is certain.
The middle term 'obligatorily acted upon' is eliminated to produce the conclusion.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Response to the Nafs al-Amr Objection
The objector also claims that rulings not corresponding to reality lead to compound ignorance. The commentator replies that 'decisive' here means not pure conjecture (zann mahd), and the evidence is based on consensus premises, ensuring correspondence in terms of obligation.
The Hanafi position does not require the ruling to match reality (nafs al-amr) in all cases; it suffices that the mujtahid is certain of its obligation.

جوابات اعتراض ثانی (1): اعتراض ثانی ثابت بالنظر الى الدلیل کو قطعی کہا ہے یہ قطعی طن محض کے مقابلہ میں ہے کہ ظن محض نہ ہو اور نطن کے مقابلہ میں قطعی عام ہے واقعہ کے مطابق ہو یانہ ہو یہ سب ظن کے خلاف ہے اور ہا ئیں معنی قطعی ہے۔ (۲):۔ یہ جو ہم نے کہا کہ ہر وہ حکم کہ جس میں ظن غالب ہو وہ قطعی ہے دلیل کی طرف نظر کرتے ہوئے تو اس میں دلیل سے مراد وہ دلیل ہے جو مقدمہ اجماعیہ سے مرکب ہو مثلاً ایک آدمی نے خبر دی کہ فلاں بادشاہ مر گیا ہے اور جس کو خبر دی گئی اس نے دیکھا کہ عورتوں نے رونا شروع کر دیا اور گھر سے نکل آئیں یہ ان کا نکلنا اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ واقعی بادشاہ مرا ہے خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ جب دلیل مقدمہ اجماعیہ سے مرکب ہوگی تو منظنون المجعد قطعی ہوگا۔

فقہ کے قطعی ہونے کے ثبوت کے لئے آخری کوشش
و غالیہ ما امکن : ۔ سے اس بات کو ثابت کرنے کے لئے کہ فقہ قطعی چیز ہے شارع نے شرح منہاج میں نقل کردہ دلیل کو بیان کیا یہ دلیل دو قیاسوں پر مشتمل ہے ۔
قیاس اول: کل حکم مظنون للجهد فقد سحب العمل علیہ قطعا یہ صغریٰ ہے اس میں کل حکم مظنون للمجتہد یہ موضوع ہے فقد يحجب العمل علیہ قطعا یہ حمول ہے اور اس کا کبری قیاس ثانی کا نتیجہ بنے گا اور کل ہنجب العمل علیه قطعا علم قطعا نہ حکم اللہ تعالیٰ یہ قیاس ثانی کا صغرئی ہے اب نتیجہ کی صحت کے لئے صغریٰ اور کبری کا دلیل ہونا ضروری ہے اب قیاس اول کے صغریٰ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ صغریٰ تھا كل حكم مظنون للمجيد فقد يجب العمل علیہ قطعا اس کی دلیل اجتماع ہے یعنی اجتماع نے ظن غالب کا اعتبار کیا ہے اور اخبار احاد متواترة المعنی بھی ظن غالب کا اعتبار کرتی ہیں گویا کہ دلیل قطعی سے ثابت ہے کیونکہ اجماع اور اخبار احاد ثابت کر رہی ہیں کہ ہر حکم جو مظنون المجتہد ہو وہ قطعی اور واجب العمل ہوتا ہے اور قیاس ثانی کے صغری وکل ما يجب العمل عالیہ قطعا علم قطعا انہ حکم ہوتا ۔ اللہ تعالیٰ اس کی دلیل عمل کا واجب ہوتا ہے کہ اگر اللہ کا حکم نہ ہوتا تو عمل بھی واجب نہ ہوتا اور کبری وکل ما علم قطعا انہ حکم اللہ تعالی فہو معلوم قطعا اب دونوں طرفوں میں حد وسط علم قطعا انہ حکم اللہ تعالیٰ ہے اس کو گرائیں گے تو نتیجہ ہو گا کل پنجب علیہ العمل قطعاً فهو معلوم قطعا اب قیاس ثانی کے نتیجہ کو قیاس اول کا کبری بنائیں مشکل اوا، یوں بنے گی (مغربی) کل حکم مظنون للمجتهد فقد يجب العمل عليه قطعاً ( كبرى ) وكل ما يجب عليه العمل قطعاً فهو معلوم قطعاً اب حد اوسط ہو گى كل ما يجب عليه العمل اس کو گرانے سے نتیجہ نکلے گا کل حکم مظنون للمجتهد فهو معلوم قطعاً لہذا جب یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ مظنون لمجند (مسائل قیاسیہ قطعی ہیں تو اس سے یہ بھی ثابت ہوگیا کہ فقہ قطعی ہے کیونکہ فقہ انہیں
It is obligatory to act upon it, and any ruling upon which action is obligatory is decisive (qat'i). Or at least the predominant conjecture (zann ghalib) of the mujtahid, so it is established by looking at the evidence. When rulings are established by evidence, they become decisive. So now qiyasi issues are decisive. Then fiqh comprises decisive matters, so it is correct to call it knowledge. Can the speculative (zanni) be called decisive? 

Then it is said: 'If it is said:' – the commentator presents an objection and then answers with 'We say:'
Objection: You said that which is conjectured by the mujtahid is decisive. This would necessitate that qiyasi issues are decisive, whereas qiyasi issues are speculative because you yourself call them conjectured. So how can a speculative thing be decisive? Because the decisive is at the level of certainty and admits no possibility of its opposite or contrary side, while the speculative thing admits the possibility of its opposite and contrary. So how can you call the speculative decisive?
Answer: This is only until ijtihad and deduction are not done. Now that qiyas and deduction have been done, these issues become decisive because the two times are different. So contradiction does not arise because qiyasi issues during the time of ijtihad and deduction are speculative, and after ijtihad and deduction they are decisive. Now we prove this by the first form (of syllogism): (Minor premise) It is known that it is conjectured by the mujtahid. (Major premise) Everything that is known to be conjectured by the mujtahid is known to be established in reality (nafs al-amr). This is the first form. Now we eliminate the middle term, so the result: It is known that it is established in reality.

But the objector: In this passage, the commentator transmits the objection when he does not deem every mujtahid correct. The matn said: Then the argument would be that from the Shariah a text is established: 'Every ruling that is predominantly conjectured by the mujtahid, it becomes obligatory to act upon it' or 'so it is established by looking at the evidence.' Now in the first case, the objection is that fiqh would be the knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, whereas fiqh is the knowledge of rulings, not the knowledge of the obligation to act. And in the second case (so it is established by looking at the evidence), the objection: If this expression is used for argument, it would necessitate that whether the ruling is established in reality or not, when it is established by looking at the evidence, it is decisive, whereas something that does not correspond to reality is compound ignorance, which cannot be decisive and certain.

Answer to the first objection: The answer to this objection is that we said: In the first case, when predominant conjecture is obtained, action is obligatory. Now the meaning of 'action is obligatory' is that the mujtahid becomes certain that this thing is obligatory, and the meaning of 'action is prohibited' is that he becomes certain that it is prohibited. So the meaning of 'obligation to act' is that he becomes certain of its being obligatory and similarly of its being prohibited. And the obligatory and prohibited are the very rulings, and certainty of them is knowledge. So in this case too, fiqh is the name for knowledge of rulings.

Answers to the second objection: (1) The second objection is that 'established by looking at the evidence' is called decisive. This decisive is in contrast to pure conjecture (zann mahd) – that it is not pure conjecture. And in contrast to conjecture, decisive is general, whether it matches reality or not. All of this is opposite to conjecture, and here the meaning of decisive is that. (2) What we said that every ruling in which predominant conjecture occurs is decisive by looking at the evidence – in this, 'evidence' refers to that evidence which is composed of premises that are matters of consensus (ijma). For example, a man informed that a certain king has died, and the one informed saw that women started wailing and came out of their homes. This coming out is evidence that the king has indeed died. In summary, when the evidence is composed of premises that are matters of consensus, then the conjectured becomes decisive.

Final attempt to prove that fiqh is decisive: 'And by way of what is possible' – to prove that fiqh is decisive, the commentator (sharih) mentioned the proof transmitted from Sharh al-Minhaj. This proof consists of two syllogisms:

First syllogism: 'Every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid is obligatorily acted upon decisively' – this is the minor premise. In it, 'every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid' is the subject, and 'obligatorily acted upon decisively' is the predicate. Its conclusion will be the major premise of the second syllogism. And 'everything that is obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively to be the ruling of Allah' – this is the minor premise of the second syllogism. Now for the soundness of the conclusion, it is necessary for the minor and major premises to have evidence. Now the evidence for the minor premise of the first syllogism: It was the minor premise: 'Every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid is obligatorily acted upon decisively.' Its evidence is consensus (ijma), because consensus has validated predominant conjecture, and also ahad hadith with cumulative meaning (mutawatir bi al-ma'na) validate predominant conjecture. Thus it is established by decisive evidence because consensus and ahad hadith prove that every ruling that is conjectured by the mujtahid is decisive and obligatory to act upon. And for the minor premise of the second syllogism: 'Everything that is obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively to be the ruling of Allah' – its evidence is that action is obligatory, because if it were not Allah's ruling, action would not be obligatory. And the major premise: 'And everything that is known decisively to be the ruling of Allah is known decisively.' So now eliminating the middle term between the two sides, which is 'known decisively to be the ruling of Allah', we get the result: 'Everything that is obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively.' Then taking the result of the second syllogism as the major premise of the first syllogism, the combined syllogism becomes: (Minor) Every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid is obligatorily acted upon decisively. (Major) Everything that is obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively. Now the middle term is 'everything that is obligatorily acted upon'. Eliminating it, the result is: 'Every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid is known decisively.' Therefore, when it is established that the conjectured (qiyasi issues) are decisive, then it is also established that fiqh is decisive, because fiqh is these very rulings.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a defence of the Hanafi position that fiqh (jurisprudence) is a certain science ('ilm qat'i) despite being derived through speculative reasoning (zann). The author, a commentator on al-Tawdih, addresses an objection: if fiqh rulings are based on the predominant conjecture (zann ghalib) of the mujtahid, how can they be called decisive (qat'i)? The response distinguishes between two temporal stages: before ijtihad, the ruling is speculative; after ijtihad, the mujtahid attains certainty regarding the ruling's obligation, and thus it becomes qat'i for him. This is not contradictory because the two times differ. The author then presents a formal proof using two syllogisms. The first syllogism is: every ruling conjectured by the mujtahid is obligatorily acted upon decisively (proven by ijma and ahad hadith). The second: everything obligatorily acted upon decisively is known decisively to be Allah's ruling (since obligation implies divine command). Combining yields: every conjectured ruling is known decisively – thus fiqh is certain. The commentator also clarifies that 'decisive' here means not pure conjecture, and that the evidence used is composed of premises accepted by consensus (muqaddimat ijma'iyya). This argument reinforces the Hanafi view that fiqh qualifies as knowledge ('ilm) even though it relies on ijtihad, because the shari'ah itself mandates acting on predominant conjecture.

Key Terms
ظن غالب (zann ghalib): Predominant conjecture; a strong probability that is sufficient for action in Islamic law.
قطع (qat'i): Decisive; a ruling that is certain and admits no possibility of its opposite, at least in terms of being binding for the mujtahid.
مسائل قیاسیہ (masa'il qiyasiyya): Rulings derived through analogical reasoning (qiyas) by the mujtahid.
نفس الأمر (nafs al-amr): The reality; the actual ruling in the divine knowledge regardless of human perception.
اخبار احاد متواترة المعنى (akhbar ahad mutawatirat al-ma'na): Hadith reports transmitted singly but with cumulative meaning that reaches the level of certainty in meaning.
شکل اول (shakl awwal): The first figure of Aristotelian syllogism; a valid syllogistic form used in Islamic logic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main evidence that a conjectured ruling (maznun al-mujtahid) is obligatorily acted upon decisively?
A) The Quranic verse on obedience to the Prophet
B) Consensus (ijma) and ahad hadith with cumulative meaning
C) The principle that every ruling is either obligatory or prohibited
D) The fact that the mujtahid is always correct

Key Takeaway
After ijtihad, the mujtahid's predominant conjecture becomes certain in obligation, making fiqh a decisive science, supported by consensus and ahad hadith with cumulative meaning.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
12 min
Final Proof that Fiqh is Certain (Qat'i)
Proving fiqh certainty via two syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two-syllogism argument to demonstrate that a fiqh ruling derived from predominant probability (ẓann ghālib) is certain (qaṭ‘ī) in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a probable ruling becomes certain when backed by ijmā‘ and tawātur ma‘nawī.

This lesson tackles a central epistemological claim: that fiqh rulings, though based on speculative reasoning, are ultimately certain. The author presents a sophisticated compound syllogism to prove that every ruling which is probable (maẓnūn) for the mujtahid is known with certainty to be Allah’s law. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Hanafi position on the authority of ijtihād and the binding nature of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component syllogisms
Break the argument into its two qiyās: the first establishes that every probable ruling is necessarily acted upon; the second establishes that everything acted upon is certainly known as Allah's ruling.
The middle term of the compound syllogism is 'known certainly to be Allah's ruling'.
2
Locate the evidence for the first syllogism's minor premise
The minor premise 'every probable ruling is necessarily acted upon' is proven by ijmā‘ (consensus) and āḥād akhbār with tawātur ma‘nawī. Both confirm that predominant probability (ẓann ghālib) demands action.
This is the key step where speculative evidence is elevated to certainty.
3
Trace the proof of the second syllogism's minor premise
The minor 'everything necessitating action is known certainly as Allah's ruling' is proven by the very obligation: if it were not Allah's command, action would not be obligatory. Obligation implies divine origin.
This hinges on the necessary connection between divine command and obligation.
4
Derive the final conclusion by eliminating the middle term
Combine the conclusions: first syllogism yields 'probable rulings necessitate action'; second syllogism yields 'whatever necessitates action is known certainly'. Dropping the middle term gives 'every probable ruling is known certainly'.
Thus, ẓannī rulings are qaṭ‘ī in themselves, even though the path (ṭarīq) involves speculation.
5
Apply the conclusion to fiqh
Since fiqh consists precisely of these qiyāsī (analogical) rulings, fiqh as a whole is certain. Probability concerns only the method, not the result.
This resolves the apparent contradiction between speculative evidence and definitive rulings.

و غالیہ ما امکن : ۔ سے اس بات کو ثابت کرنے کے لئے کہ فقہ قطعی چیز ہے شارع نے شرح منہاج میں نقل کردہ دلیل کو بیان کیا یہ دلیل دو قیاسوں پر مشتمل ہے ۔
قیاس اول: کل حکم مظنون للجهد فقد سحب العمل علیہ قطعا یہ صغریٰ ہے اس میں کل حکم مظنون للمجتہد یہ موضوع ہے فقد يحجب العمل علیہ قطعا یہ حمول ہے اور اس کا کبری قیاس ثانی کا نتیجہ بنے گا اور کل ہنجب العمل علیه قطعا علم قطعا نہ حکم اللہ تعالیٰ یہ قیاس ثانی کا صغرئی ہے اب نتیجہ کی صحت کے لئے صغریٰ اور کبری کا دلیل ہونا ضروری ہے اب قیاس اول کے صغریٰ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ صغریٰ تھا كل حكم مظنون للمجيد فقد يجب العمل علیہ قطعا اس کی دلیل اجتماع ہے یعنی اجتماع نے ظن غالب کا اعتبار کیا ہے اور اخبار احاد متواترة المعنی بھی ظن غالب کا اعتبار کرتی ہیں گویا کہ دلیل قطعی سے ثابت ہے کیونکہ اجماع اور اخبار احاد ثابت کر رہی ہیں کہ ہر حکم جو مظنون المجتہد ہو وہ قطعی اور واجب العمل ہوتا ہے اور قیاس ثانی کے صغری وکل ما يجب العمل عالیہ قطعا علم قطعا انہ حکم ہوتا ۔ اللہ تعالیٰ اس کی دلیل عمل کا واجب ہوتا ہے کہ اگر اللہ کا حکم نہ ہوتا تو عمل بھی واجب نہ ہوتا اور کبری وکل ما علم قطعا انہ حکم اللہ تعالی فہو معلوم قطعا اب دونوں طرفوں میں حد وسط علم قطعا انہ حکم اللہ تعالیٰ ہے اس کو گرائیں گے تو نتیجہ ہو گا کل پنجب علیہ العمل قطعاً فهو معلوم قطعا اب قیاس ثانی کے نتیجہ کو قیاس اول کا کبری بنائیں مشکل اوا، یوں بنے گی (مغربی) کل حکم مظنون للمجتهد فقد يجب العمل عليه قطعاً ( كبرى ) وكل ما يجب عليه العمل قطعاً فهو معلوم قطعاً اب حد اوسط ہو گى كل ما يجب عليه العمل اس کو گرانے سے نتیجہ نکلے گا کل حکم مظنون للمجتهد فهو معلوم قطعاً لہذا جب یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ مظنون لمجند (مسائل قیاسیہ قطعی ہیں تو اس سے یہ بھی ثابت ہوگیا کہ فقہ قطعی ہے کیونکہ فقہ انہیں سائل قیاسیہ کا نام ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ ظن طریق میں ہے (واللہ اعلم بالصواب)۔
And Ghāliyā mā amkan: To prove that fiqh is a certain thing, the commentator (shāriḥ) mentioned the proof quoted in Sharḥ al-Minhāj. This proof consists of two syllogisms. First syllogism: Every ruling that is probable (maẓnūn) for the mujtahid – then action upon it is necessary certainly. This is the minor premise. Its subject is 'every ruling probable for the mujtahid' and its predicate is 'then action upon it is necessary certainly'. Its major premise will be the conclusion of the second syllogism. And 'everything that is necessary to act upon certainly is known certainly to be the ruling of Allah' – this is the minor of the second syllogism. Now for the validity of the conclusion, it is necessary that the minor and major be proven. The proof of the minor of the first syllogism – which was 'every ruling probable for the mujtahid then action upon it is necessary certainly' – its proof is ijmā‘ (consensus), i.e., consensus gave weight to predominant probability, and also āḥād akhbār with tawātur in meaning give weight to predominant probability. So it is proven by a certain proof because ijmā‘ and āḥād akhbār establish that every ruling that is probable for the mujtahid is certain and necessary to act upon. And the proof of the minor of the second syllogism – 'everything that is necessary to act upon certainly is known certainly to be the ruling of Allah' – is that action is obligatory, for if it were not the ruling of Allah, action would not be obligatory. And the major is 'everything that is known certainly to be the ruling of Allah is known certainly'. Now the middle term on both sides is 'known certainly to be the ruling of Allah'. When we drop it, the result is: everything that is necessary to act upon is known certainly. Then making the conclusion of the second syllogism the major of the first syllogism: (minor) every ruling probable for the mujtahid then action upon it is necessary certainly. (major) everything that is necessary to act upon is known certainly. The middle term becomes 'everything that is necessary to act upon'. Dropping it, the result is: every ruling probable for the mujtahid is known certainly. So when it is established that what is probable for the mujtahid (qiyāsī issues) are certain, then it is also established that fiqh is certain, because fiqh is the name of these qiyāsī issues. So it becomes known that probability is in the path (not in the ruling). And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the final and most rigorous attempt (ghāliyā mā amkan) to demonstrate that the rulings of fiqh, though arrived at through speculative reasoning (ijtihād), are in fact certain (qaṭ‘ī). The author constructs a compound syllogism (qiyās) to prove that every ruling that is deemed probable (maẓnūn) by the mujtahid is actually known with certainty to be Allah's ruling. 

The first syllogism establishes the minor premise: 'Every ruling probable for the mujtahid is necessarily acted upon certainly.' This is proven by ijmā‘ (consensus) and āḥād akhbār that carry tawātur in meaning—both of which give authoritative weight to predominant probability (ghālib al-ẓann). The second syllogism aims to prove that every ruling that is necessarily acted upon is known certainly to be Allah's rule. The author argues that if it were not Allah's rule, the action would not be obligatory—meaning obligation implies divine command. 

The middle term of the compound syllogism is 'known certainly to be Allah's ruling', and by eliminating it, the conclusion emerges: every probable ruling is known certainly. Therefore, fiqh, which consists of these qiyāsī (analogical) rulings, is certain. The author notes that probability (ẓann) belongs to the path of reasoning (ṭarīq), not to the ruling itself. This discussion is rooted in the Hanafi position that, despite the apparent reliance on speculative evidence, the resulting fiqh rulings are certain in themselves because they are backed by binding sources (consensus, widely transmitted meanings). The passage also sets the stage for Taftazānī's critique in the following section.

Key Terms
قطعی (qaṭ‘ī): Certain, definitive; a ruling that is known beyond doubt to be Allah's command.
ظن غالب (ẓann ghālib): Predominant probability; a high degree of certainty short of absolute, considered authoritative in Hanafi method.
اجماع (ijmā‘): Consensus of the mujtahidīn of the ummah on a ruling.
قیاس مرکب (qiyās murakkab): Compound syllogism; a logical argument combining two or more syllogisms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes a ruling that is ẓannī (probable) become certain (qaṭ‘ī)?
A) The personal authority of the mujtahid
B) Consensus (ijmā‘) and āḥād akhbār with tawātur ma‘nawī
C) The use of explicit qiyās (analogy)
D) The acceptance of the layperson

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a ruling derived from predominant probability (ẓann ghālib) is considered certain (qaṭ‘ī) when that probability is supported by ijmā‘ and tawātur ma‘nawī.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
The Four Sources of Fiqh and Examples of Qiyas from Each
Defining the four sources and exemplifying qiyas from each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four sources of fiqh in Hanafi usul, explain the status of qiyas as a manifestation (muzhir) rather than an independent establisher, and derive an analogy from a primary source by analyzing the illah.

Core Idea
Qiyas does not create new rulings; it manifests rulings already implicit in the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma through a shared cause.

In this passage, the author defines the principles of fiqh (usul al-fiqh) as four: the Book, the Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas. The first three are absolute sources that independently establish rulings. Qiyas, however, is a derived source: it is a principle in that it produces rulings, but it is a branch because its cause (illah) is extracted from the primary texts. The passage then provides concrete examples of qiyas from each source, and addresses a nuanced objection about redundancy. This lesson will walk you through the conceptual framework and the analytical steps for identifying and critiquing qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four sources and their hierarchy
Distinguish usul mutlaqah (Book, Sunnah, Ijma) from usul nāqis (Qiyas). Understand why qiyas is considered a branch: it depends on the primary sources for its cause (illah).
Qiyas is 'asl min wajh wa far' min wajh' (a principle in one respect, a branch in another).
2
Examine the examples of qiyas from each source
For each example (Quran: sodomy from menstruation; Sunnah: gypsum from wheat; Ijma: mother of fornicated woman from mother of slave), identify the original case (asl), the new case (far'), and the shared cause (illah).
The illah for the Quran example is 'adha' (harm). For the Sunnah example, it is measurement/genus. For Ijma, it is unlawful intercourse causing in-law prohibition.
3
Analyse the objection and response
The objection: sodomy is already textually prohibited via the story of Lot, so qiyas is unnecessary. The Hanafi response: (1) the qiyas is specific to sodomy with one's wife, which might be doubted; (2) qiyas serves as additional emphasis (tawkid).
This shows that qiyas can reinforce existing rulings, not just derive new ones.
4
Apply the Hanafi definition of qiyas
Use the legal_definition_dissector to confirm that qiyas is a manifestation (muzhir) of rulings, not an independent establisher. Check the conditions (shurut) and preventers (mawani').
Qiyas is valid only when there is no direct text (nass) on the new case, or to clarify an ambiguous text.

چار چیزیں ہیں (۱) کتاب اللہ (۲) سنت رسول الله الله (۳) اجماع امت محمدیہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم (۴) قیاس شرعی فرق ان میں یہ ہے کہ پہلے تین اصول مطلقہ ہیں اور چوتھا اصل قیاس یہ اصل ناقص ہے یعنی مین وجہ اصل ہے شرائع من قبلنا کے احکام جو قرآن پاک میں مذکور ہیں اور ان کا رد نہ کیا گیا ہو تو وہ امت محمدیہ پر لازم ہیں لہذا قوم لوط کا تذکرہ قرآن پاک میں موجود ہے اور ان کا عیب بیان کیا گیا ہے اور اس کی حرمت کو قرآن مجید سے ثابت ہے لیکن لوطی کے انکار کو ختم کرنے کے لئے اور مزید تاکید کے لئے اس کو قیاس کی مثال بنایا ہے کہ جس طرح اس کی حرمت نص سے ثابت ہے اسی طرح قیاس منصوص العلہ سے بھی اس کی حرمت ثابت ہے قیاس مستقبط من الحدیث کی مثال جس طرح چونے کے ایک قفیز کی بیع کی حرمت دو قفیز کے
… the menses, and the cause is harm. And as for what is derived from the Sunnah, it is like the analogy of the prohibition of one qafiz of gypsum for two qafiz upon the prohibition of one qafiz of wheat for two qafiz, established by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and excess is usury.' And as for what is derived from consensus, they gave as a similar example the analogy of unlawful intercourse upon lawful intercourse in the prohibition of in-law relationship, meaning the analogy of the prohibition of intercourse with the mother of the woman with whom one has committed fornication upon the prohibition of intercourse with the mother of his female slave whom he had intercourse with, and the prohibition in the analogue is established by consensus, and there is no text in it, rather the text came concerning the mothers of women without the condition of intercourse. Translation: The principles of fiqh are the Book, the Sunnah of the Messenger, consensus, and analogy, even if analogy is a branch of these three. When it was mentioned that the principles of fiqh are those upon which fiqh depends, the intention was to state what fiqh depends on. So he said that they are these four. The first three are absolute principles because each of them establishes a ruling. As for analogy, it is a principle in one respect because it relates to the ruling, and a branch in another respect because it is a branch of the first three principles, since the cause in it is derived from the contexts of those three. Thus the ruling established by analogy is established by those proofs, and it is not an establisher but rather a manifestation. As for an example of analogy derived from the Book: like the analogy of the prohibition of sodomy upon the prohibition of intercourse during menses, which is established by Allah's saying: 'Say: It is harm, so keep away from women during menses.' The cause is the harm. As for an example of analogy derived from the Sunnah: like the analogy of the prohibition of one qafiz of gypsum for two qafiz upon the prohibition of one qafiz of wheat for two qafiz, from that which is established by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like, hand to hand, and excess is usury.' As for an example of that derived from consensus: they gave as its example the analogy of unlawful intercourse upon lawful intercourse in the prohibition of in-law relationship, meaning the analogy of the prohibition of intercourse with the mother of the fornicated woman upon the prohibition of intercourse with the mother of his female slave whom he had intercourse with. The prohibition in the analogue is established by consensus, and there is no text in it, rather the text came concerning the mothers of women without the condition of intercourse. The referents of usul al-fiqh: The author first defined usul al-fiqh as that upon which fiqh is based. Now he states its referents: it consists of four things: (1) the Book of Allah, (2) the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, (3) the consensus of the Ummah of Muhammad, peace be upon him, and (4) legal analogy. The difference among them is that the first three are absolute principles, and the fourth, analogy, is an incomplete principle, meaning it is a principle in one respect and a branch in another. If one looks at the ruling established by analogy, in that respect it is a principle; and if one looks at the derivation of its cause from the three principles, in that respect it is a branch, because the ruling established by analogy is in reality established from the three proofs; analogy is only a manifestation of rulings, not an establisher. Examples of analogy derived from the three principles: An example of analogy from the Book: sodomy is prohibited; it was analogized to the prohibition of intercourse with one's wife during menses, because its cause is harm. Therefore it is also prohibited due to this cause. This analogy is derived from the Book. Question: The prohibition of sodomy is established from the Book, i.e., from the text, because the rule is that the laws of those before us that are mentioned in the Qur'an and not abrogated are binding on this Ummah. The mention of the people of Lot in the Qur'an and the condemnation of their deed, without abrogation, indicates that sodomy is prohibited for them and thus for us. This issue is textually established, not by analogy, because analogy applies to matters not textually established. So making it an example of analogy is incorrect. Answer (1): The Qur'an mentions sodomy absolutely, whether with men or women. Here the analogy concerns sodomy with one's wife, because one might think that all forms of benefit with one's wife are permissible, so sodomy might be permissible. Thus it is said that sodomy with one's wife is also prohibited, analogized to the prohibition of intercourse during menses due to harm. Answer (2): It is correct that the prohibition of sodomy is established from the Qur'an, but to remove doubt and for additional emphasis, it was made an example of analogy. Just as its prohibition is textually established, similarly its prohibition is established by analogy with a textually specified cause. An example of analogy derived from hadith: just as the prohibition of selling one qafiz of gypsum for two qafiz …
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of usul al-fiqh and the sources of Islamic law. The author states that the principles of fiqh are four: the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of the Prophet, consensus of the Ummah, and legal analogy (qiyas). The first three are absolute principles (usul mutlaqah) because they independently establish rulings. Qiyas, however, is both a principle and a branch: it is a principle in that it produces rulings, but it is a branch because its validity and its cause (illah) are derived from the three primary sources. Thus, qiyas does not create new rulings; it merely manifests rulings that are implicit in the primary sources. The passage then provides concrete examples of qiyas from each source. From the Quran: the prohibition of sodomy (liwat) with one's wife is analogized to the prohibition of intercourse during menstruation, with the common cause (illah) being 'harm' (adha). From the Sunnah: the prohibition of exchanging one qafiz of gypsum for two qafiz is analogized to the prohibition of exchanging one qafiz of wheat for two qafiz, based on the hadith prohibiting usury in wheat exchanges. From Ijma: the prohibition of marrying the mother of a woman with whom one has committed fornication is analogized to the prohibition of marrying the mother of one's female slave after having intercourse with her, a ruling established by consensus despite the absence of a direct text. The passage then addresses a potential objection: if sodomy is already prohibited by the Quran (via the story of Lot's people), why use it as an example of qiyas? The answer clarifies that the qiyas is specifically for sodomy with one's wife, which might be doubted, and serves as additional emphasis (tawkid). This demonstrates that qiyas can be used to reinforce textual rulings, not just to derive new ones.

Key Terms
قياس (Qiyas): A legal analogy that extends a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a shared cause (illah).
اصل (Asl): In this context, it means a primary source or principle of fiqh (Book, Sunnah, Ijma) or the original case in an analogy.
علة (Illah): The effective cause or attribute that is common to the original and new cases, on which the analogy is based.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the absolute principles (usul mutlaqah)?
A) The Book of Allah
B) The Sunnah of the Prophet
C) The consensus of the Ummah
D) Legal analogy (qiyas)

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a manifestation of the rulings from the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma; it does not establish new rulings independently.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Qiyas as a Deficient Source: Asl Naqis in Hanafi Usul
Explains why Qiyas is deficient and the objection against it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'asl naqis' and explain why Qiyas is classified as such in Hanafi usul, distinguishing it from the absolute sources (asl mutlaq).

Core Idea
Qiyas is not an independent source; it only manifests rulings derived from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

In Hanafi legal theory, the four sources of law are not equal in status. The first three—Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma—are considered absolute and independent. Qiyas, however, is classified as a deficient source (asl naqis). This lesson unpacks why that distinction matters and how it shapes the hierarchy of evidence in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of sources
Recognize that the four sources are divided into absolute (asl mutlaq) and deficient (asl naqis). The first three are absolute; Qiyas is deficient.
This division is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
Define 'asl mutlaq'
An absolute source is one that is not derived from another source. It stands independently as a foundation for rulings.
Examples: Quran, Sunnah, Ijma.
3
Define 'asl naqis'
A deficient source is one that is an origin from one perspective (since it is used to derive rulings) but a branch from another (since its basis comes from the absolute sources).
Qiyas is asl naqis because it only manifests rulings, not establish them.
4
Apply to Qiyas
Analyze why Qiyas fits the definition: its ruling is attributed to the absolute sources, and it only extends the known ruling to new cases via a common illah.
The illah must come from the three absolute sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول اربعہ میں مراتب کے اعتبار سے فرق
ثم السلتہ الاول : یہ شرح کی عبارت کا چوتھا حصہ ہے اس عبارت میں شاریخ نے متن کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے اصول اربعہ کا فرق بیان کیا ہے کہ پہلے تین اصول اصل مطلق ہیں اور چوتھا اصل یعنی قیاس اصل ناقص ہے یہ من وجہ اصل ہے اور من وجہ فرع ہے اگر حکم کی طرف دیکھیں کہ اس کا ظاہری سبب قیاس ہے تو یہ اصل ہے اور اگر اس کو دیکھیں کہ اس کی علت اصول ثلثہ سے مستلبطہ ہے تو یہ فرع ہے کیونکہ حکم در اصل انہی دلائل ثلاثہ کی طرف منسوب ہوتا ہے اور قیاس کا اثر صرف حکم کو ظاہر کرنے میں ثابت ہوتا ہے اور حکم کو خصوص سے عموم کی طرف تبدیل کرنے میں ظاہر ہوتا ہے حکم کو ثابت کرنے میں ظاہر نہیں ہوتا اس لئے یہ اصل ناقص ہے اور قیاس کے اصل ناقص ہونے کی وجہ سے اصولین نے جب اصول اربعہ کو بیان کیا تو پہلے تین کو علیحدہ بیان کیا اور قیاس کو علیحدہ ذکر کیا اور عنوان یوں قائم کیا اصول الفقہ ثلثۃ الکتاب والسنۃ والا جماع والاصل الرابع القياس الخ کیونکہ قیاس اصل ضعیف ہے اس لئے اسے علیحدہ الاصل الرابع القیاس کے عنوان سے ذکر کرتے ہیں۔ وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَيْهِ بِوُجُوهِ الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّهُ لَا مَعنَى لِلَّأصلِ المُطلَقِ إِلَّا مَا يُبَتَنَى عَلَيهِ غَيْرُهُ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ فَرعًا لِشَيْءٍ آخَرَ أَو لَم يَكُن وَلِهَذَا صَحَّ إِطَلَاقُهُ عَلَى الْآبِ وَإِن
Difference in status among the four sources. Then the first characteristic: This is the fourth part of the commentary. In this, the commentator explains the difference among the four sources: the first three are absolute sources (asl mutlaq), while the fourth, qiyas, is a deficient source (asl naqis). It is a source from one perspective and a branch from another. If we look at the ruling whose apparent cause is qiyas, it is an origin; but if we look at the cause derived from the three sources, it is a branch. Because the ruling is ultimately attributed to those three proofs, and qiyas only manifests the ruling, extending it from specific to general, not establishing it. Hence it is a deficient source. For this reason, when usul scholars mention the four, they state: 'The principles of fiqh are three: the Book, the Sunnah, and Ijma; and the fourth principle is qiyas...' because qiyas is a weak source. Objection was raised to this on several grounds: first, there is no meaning of absolute source except that upon which something else is built, whether it itself is a branch of something else or not, hence it correctly applies to a father even though he is also a son...
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the status of Qiyas within the Hanafi usul hierarchy. The first three sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma) are considered absolute (asl mutlaq) because they are independent and foundational. Qiyas, however, is a deficient source (asl naqis) because it does not establish a ruling on its own; it only manifests and extends rulings derived from the other three. It is called a source only because rulings are attributed to it in a derivative sense (min wajh). This is why many usul works list the first three as the principles and then add 'and the fourth principle is qiyas' separately. The passage also begins to discuss an objection: that the definition of 'absolute source' (asl mutlaq) should apply to anything upon which something else is built, even if it itself is derived from something else (like a father who is also a son). This objection is from the matn (Tawdih) and is being addressed in the commentary. The Hanafi position is that Qiyas is inherently dependent, hence deficient, which is a key distinguishing feature of Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
اصل مطلق (asl mutlaq): An independent, absolute source that is not derived from another source. Applied to Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.
اصل ناقص (asl naqis): A deficient source that is considered an origin from one aspect but a branch from another. Qiyas is such.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafi usul scholars call Qiyas a 'deficient source' (asl naqis)?
A) Because it is rarely used
B) Because it does not establish a ruling but only manifests and extends it
C) Because it is weaker than Ijma
D) Because it requires ijtihad

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a deficient source (asl naqis) because it relies on absolute sources for its illah and only manifests rulings rather than establishing them.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Qiyas as a Deficient Principle: Objections and Hanafi Responses
Defense of qiyas as a deficient principle via refutation of five objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that qiyas is a deficient principle (asl naqis) and evaluate the five objections raised against this classification using the responses provided.

Core Idea
Qiyas is both a principle and a branch; its deficiency lies in its dependence on the three absolute sources.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi defense of classifying qiyas as a deficient source. The objectors argue that qiyas should be considered an absolute principle like the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma. The Hanafi responses clarify that originality is a matter of degree and that qiyas does not establish rulings independently but manifests the rulings of the primary sources. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of the epistemological hierarchy in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Classification
Recognize that Hanafis divide the sources into three absolute (Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma) and one deficient (qiyas). The term 'asl mutlaq' applies to the first three, while qiyas is 'asl naqis' because it is both a principle (in causing a ruling) and a branch (in deriving its illa from other sources).
This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul; it affects how qiyas is cited in fatwa.
2
Understand the Five Objections
Each objection challenges the logic of treating qiyas as deficient. (1) The father analogy: a father is both a principle (for his child) and a branch (of his own father). (2) Proximate cause deserves the name more than remote cause. (3) In a division, the weak part need not be singled out. (4) Changing a ruling from specific to general implies establishing it anew, which is absolute originality. (5) Ijma also depends on a source, so it should not be absolute.
These objections come from unspecified critics; the Hanafi responses address each sequentially.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Responses
For each objection, see how the response defends the classification. (1) 'Principle' is said by degrees (tashkik); the weaker does not qualify as absolute. (2) The proximate cause actually acts on its branch, but qiyas does not establish the ruling—it only shows reliance on text/Ijma. (3) In divisions of real essences, no part is inherently superior. The responses so far cover the first three objections; the response to the fourth and fifth is cut off.
The concept of tashkik is key: originality is a graded term, not univocal.
4
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Recognize the practical consequence: qiyas is treated as a secondary tool, not an independent source. In fatwa, a Hanafi jurist cannot rely on qiyas alone without linking it to one of the three absolute sources. This affects the strength of arguments in istinbat.
This dispute is not merely academic; it governs how rulings are derived and contested.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل عقلی بھی ہے جس کا درجہ قیاس کے بعد ہے جس کو معقول کا نام دیتے ہیں خاص طور پر صاحب ہدایہ دلیل عقلی دیتے ہیں اور یہ حجت بن جاتی ہے خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ دلائل شرعیہ کو چار میں منحصر کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔ جواب دیا کہ حصر چار میں صحیح ہے کیونکہ پہلی امتوں کی شریعت قرآن میں بیان کی گئی ہوگی یا حدیث میں اگر قرآن پاک میں بیان کی گئی ہے تو قرآن میں داخل ہے اور اگر حدیث میں بیان کی گئی ہے تو سنت رسول اللہ یا کہ میں داخل ہے تو یہ علیحدہ دلیل شرعی نہ ہوئی اور تعامل الناس اجماع میں داخل ہے تو یہ بھی مستقل دلیل شرعی نہ ہوئی اور قول صحابی قیاس کے موافق ہوگا یا موافق نہیں ہو گا اگر قیاس کے موافق ہو تو قیاس میں داخل ہے اور اگر مخالف قیاس ہو تو صحابی نے یہ قول نبی علیہ السلام سے سن کر کیا ہوگا تو یہ قول صحابی حدیث میں داخل ہوگا کیونکہ صحابی رسول میں اپنی طرف سے کوئی شرعی بات بیان کرے یہ محال ہے لہذا یہ بھی مستقل اور ملحد و دلیل شرعی نہ ہوئی۔ باقی رہی دلیل عقلی یہ نص سے یا اجماع سے ماخوذ ہوتی ہے کیونکہ مطلقا دلیل عقلی کا اعتبار نہیں ہے جب تک کہ مؤید بالنص یا مؤید بالا جماع نہ ہو تو دلیل عقلی بھی قرآن وحدیث اور اجماع میں داخل ہوئی یہ بھی کوئی علیحدہ دلیل شرعی نہیں ہے فلا اعتراض علیہ ۔
اصول اربعہ میں مراتب کے اعتبار سے فرق
ثم السلتہ الاول : یہ شرح کی عبارت کا چوتھا حصہ ہے اس عبارت میں شاریخ نے متن کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے اصول اربعہ کا فرق بیان کیا ہے کہ پہلے تین اصول اصل مطلق ہیں اور چوتھا اصل یعنی قیاس اصل ناقص ہے یہ من وجہ اصل ہے اور من وجہ فرع ہے اگر حکم کی طرف دیکھیں کہ اس کا ظاہری سبب قیاس ہے تو یہ اصل ہے اور اگر اس کو دیکھیں کہ اس کی علت اصول ثلثہ سے مستلبطہ ہے تو یہ فرع ہے کیونکہ حکم در اصل انہی دلائل ثلاثہ کی طرف منسوب ہوتا ہے اور قیاس کا اثر صرف حکم کو ظاہر کرنے میں ثابت ہوتا ہے اور حکم کو خصوص سے عموم کی طرف تبدیل کرنے میں ظاہر ہوتا ہے حکم کو ثابت کرنے میں ظاہر نہیں ہوتا اس لئے یہ اصل ناقص ہے اور قیاس کے اصل ناقص ہونے کی وجہ سے اصولین نے جب اصول اربعہ کو بیان کیا تو پہلے تین کو علیحدہ بیان کیا اور قیاس کو علیحدہ ذکر کیا اور عنوان یوں قائم کیا اصول الفقہ ثلثۃ الکتاب والسنۃ والا جماع والاصل الرابع القياس الخ کیونکہ قیاس اصل ضعیف ہے اس لئے اسے علیحدہ الاصل الرابع القیاس کے عنوان سے ذکر کرتے ہیں۔
وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَيْهِ بِوُجُوهِ الْأَوَّلُ أَنَّهُ لَا مَعنَى لِلَّأصلِ المُطلَقِ إِلَّا مَا يُبَتَنَى عَلَيهِ غَيْرُهُ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ فَرعًا لِشَيْءٍ آخَرَ أَو لَم يَكُن وَلِهَذَا صَحَّ إِطَلَاقُهُ عَلَى الْآبِ وَإِن كَانَ فَرعًا الثَّانِي أَنَّ السَّبَبَ القَرِيبَ لِلشَّيءِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ مُسَبَّبٌ عَنِ الْبَعِيدِ أَولَى بِإِطَلَاقِ اسمِ السَّبَبِ عَلَيْهِ مِن البَعِيدِ وَإِن لَم يَكُن مُسَيَّبًا عَن شَيْءٍ آخَرَ . الثَّالِثُ أَنَّ أُولَوِيَّةَ بَعضِ الْأَقسَامِ فِى مَعنَى المَقْسَمِ لَا زِمَةٌ فِى كُلِّ قِسمَةٍ فَيَلزَمُ أَنَّ يُفْرَدُ القِسمُ الضَّعِيفُ فَيُقَالُ مَثلا الكَلِمَةُ قِسمان اسمٌ وَفِعل والقِسمُ الثَّالِثُ هُوَ الحَرفِ الرَّابِعُ أَنَّ تَعْيِيرَ الحُكمِ مِن الخُصُوصِ إِلَى العُمُومِ لَا يُمكِنُ إِلَّا بِتَقْرِيرِهِ فِي صُورَةٍ أُخرَى وَهُوَ مَعَنَى الأَصَالَةِ المُطلَقَةِ الخَامِسُ أَنَّ الإِجَمَاعَ أَيضًا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى السَّنَدِ فَيَنبَغِي أَن لَا يَكُونَ أَصَلا مُطَلَقًا وَالجَوَابُ عَن الأَوَّلِ أَنَّا لَا نَدْعِى أَنَّ لِعَدَمِ الفَرعِيَّةِ دَخَلًا فِي مَفهُومِ الأصلِ بَلْ إِنَّ الأصلَ مَقُولٌ بِالتَّشْكِيكِ وَإِنَّ الأصلَ الَّذِي يَستَقِلُّ فِي مَعنَى الْأَصَالَةِ وَابْتِنَاءِ الفرع عَلَيْهِ كَالكِتَابِ مَثَلًا أَقوَى مِن الأصلِ الَّذِي يَبْتَنِي فِي ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ بِحَيثُ يَكُونُ فَرعُهُ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ مُبتَنِيًّا عَلَى ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ كَالقِيَاسِ وَالأضْعَفُ غَيْرُ دَاخِلٍ فِى الأصلِ المُطلَقِ بِمَعْنَى الكَامِلِ فِي الْأَصَالَةِ وَهَذَا بَيِّنٌ . وَأَمَّا الأبُ فَإِنَّمَا يَتَنِي عَلَى أَبِيهِ فِي الوُجُودِ لَا فِي الْأبُوَّةِ وَالأصَالَةِ لِلوَلَدِ فَلا يَكُونُ مِمَّا ذَكَرنَا فِي شَيْءٍ وَعَن الثَّانِي أَنَّ السَّبَبَ القَريب هُوَ المُؤكِّرُ فِى فَرعِهِ وَالمُفضِى إِلَيْهِ وَأَثَرُ البَعِيدِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِى الوَاسِطَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ السَّبَبُ القَرِيبُ لَا فِي فَرعِهِ فَبِالضَّرُورَةِ يَكُونُ أَولَى وَأَقوَى مِن البَعِيدِ فِي مَعنَى السَّبَبِيَّةِ وَالْأَصَالَةِ لِذَلِكَ الفَرعِ وَفِيمَا نَحْنُ فِيهِ القِيَاسُ لَيسَ بِمُثبِتٍ لِحُكمِ الفَرعِ فَضَلا عَن أَن يَكُونَ قَرِيبًا لِيَكُونَ أُولَى بِالْأَصَالَةِ بَل هُوَ مُظهِرٌ لاستنَادِ حُكمِ الفَرعِ إِلَى النَّصَّ وَالإِجماع وَعَن الثَّالِثِ أَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ لُزُومَ أَولَوِيَّةِ بَعضِ الأقسَامِ فِي كُلِّ تَقسِيمٍ وَكَيْفَ يُتَصَوَّرُ ذَلِكَ فِي تَقسِيمِ المَاهِيَّاتِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ إِلى أَنوَاعِهَا وَأَفَرَادِهَا كَتَقسِيمِ الحَيَوَانِ إِلَى
And it was objected with several aspects: First, that there is no meaning to 'absolute principle' except that which is built upon by other things, whether it is a branch of another thing or not, and thus its application to the father is valid even though he is a branch. Second, that the proximate cause of a thing, even though it is caused by a remote cause, is more deserving of the name 'cause' than the remote cause, even if it is not caused by another thing. Third, that the superiority of some parts in the meaning of the whole is not necessary in every division, so it would be required to single out the weak part, for example saying: 'The word is two parts: noun and verb, and the third part is the particle.' Fourth, that changing a ruling from particularity to generality is not possible except by establishing it in another form, and that is the meaning of absolute originality. Fifth, that Ijma also depends on a source, so it should not be an absolute principle. The answer to the first: We do not claim that non-branchhood has a role in the concept of 'principle'; rather, 'principle' is said by analogy [tashkik], and the principle that is independent in the sense of originality and the building of a branch upon it, like the Book for example, is stronger than the principle that relies in that meaning on something else, so that its branch is in reality built upon that other thing, like qiyas. And the weaker is not included in the absolute principle in the sense of perfect originality, and this is clear. As for the father, he only relies on his father in existence, not in fatherhood and originality for the child, so he is not included in what we have mentioned. And regarding the second: The proximate cause is the one that acts upon its branch and leads to it, and the effect of the remote cause is only upon the intermediary which is the proximate cause, not upon its branch. So necessarily it is more deserving and stronger in the meaning of causality and originality for that branch. And in what we are discussing, qiyas does not establish the ruling of the branch, let alone being proximate to be more deserving of originality, rather it reveals the reliance of the branch's ruling upon the text and ijma. And regarding the third: We do not accept the necessity of superiority of some parts in every division. How can that be conceived in the division of real essences into their species and individuals, like the division of animal into...
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a detailed scholastic defense of the Hanafi position that qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a deficient principle (asl naqis) among the four sources of law. The commentary first explains in Urdu that the first three (Kitab, Sunnah, Ijma) are absolute principles, while qiyas is both a principle and a branch depending on perspective. Then it presents five objections (اعتراضات) against this classification, followed by responses to the first three (the response to the third is cut off). The objections challenge: (1) the definition of 'absolute principle' using the father analogy; (2) the preference of remote over proximate cause; (3) the necessity of singling out the weak part in a division; (4) the impossibility of changing ruling from specific to general without establishing it in another form; (5) that Ijma also needs a source so it should not be absolute. The responses argue that originality is graded (tashkik) and the weaker does not qualify as absolute; that qiyas does not establish the ruling but only shows reliance on text/Ijma; and that in real divisions no part is inherently superior. This is a classic Hanafi usul debate on the epistemological status of qiyas.

Key Terms
اصل مطلق (asl mutlaq): An absolute principle that is independent and not a branch of another source in the sense of legal originality.
اصل ناقص (asl naqis): A deficient principle that is both a principle (in that it is a source of rulings) and a branch (because its illa comes from other sources).
تشكيك (tashkik): A term applied by degrees of strength; here used to argue that 'principle' is not a univocal term but one of analogy where some instances are stronger than others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the first objection, why does the father analogy fail to prove that qiyas is an absolute principle?
A) Because a father is not a principle at all.
B) Because a father is a branch only in existence, not in legal originality for his child.
C) Because the father analogy is not mentioned in the text.
D) Because qiyas is stronger than a father.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, qiyas is a deficient principle whose rulings are ultimately attributed to the three absolute sources, and its classification as asl naqis is defended by the graded nature of the term 'principle' (tashkik).
