Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Refuting Objections to 'Asl Mutlaq: The Dependent Nature of Qiyas
Response to five objections against the definition of absolute principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five objections against 'asl mutlaq and state the Hanafi responses that clarify why qiyas is not an absolute principle.

Core Idea
Qiyas depends on an original; only direct textual sources are 'asl mutlaq.

This passage presents five rational objections to the Hanafi definition of 'asl mutlaq as an independent source. The author systematically refutes each, showing that qiyas, unlike the Quran and Ijma, is inherently dependent and cannot be elevated to absolute originality. Understanding this debate sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi usul classifies sources and their evidentiary weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five objections
Read the passage and list each objection: (1) father analogy, (2) proximate cause priority, (3) necessity of singling out the strong division, (4) qiyas needed to extend rulings, (5) Ijma also needs a basis.
These objections challenge the exclusivity of 'asl mutlaq.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to each objection
For each objection, note the key rebuttal: (1) 'Asl is gradational; father analogy fails. (2) Qiyas doesn't establish the branch ruling directly. (3) Not all divisions require priority. (4) Qiyas reveals, not establishes. (5) Ijma differs in evidentiary independence and adds certainty.
Responses rely on distinctions between metaphysical and epistemological originality.
3
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: qiyas is not an independent source; its rulings are zanni and require a basis from Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma.
This affects how jurists rank evidence and derive rulings.
4
Compare with Ijma
Note how the author distinguishes Ijma from qiyas: Ijma needs a basis for its verification but not for its evidentiary value, and it adds certainty.
This reinforces the independence of Ijma as an 'asl mutlaq.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
Summarize: 'Asl mutlaq is independent and complete in originality; qiyas is dependent and incomplete. The five objections are refuted by clarifying the nature of causality, division, and evidentiary independence.
This is a cornerstone of Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Source Text

كَانَ فَرعًا الثَّانِي أَنَّ السَّبَبَ القَرِيبَ لِلشَّيءِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ مُسَبَّبٌ عَنِ الْبَعِيدِ أَولَى بِإِطَلَاقِ اسمِ السَّبَبِ عَلَيْهِ مِن البَعِيدِ وَإِن لَم يَكُن مُسَيَّبًا عَن شَيْءٍ آخَرَ . الثَّالِثُ أَنَّ أُولَوِيَّةَ بَعضِ الْأَقسَامِ فِى مَعنَى المَقْسَمِ لَا زِمَةٌ فِى كُلِّ قِسمَةٍ فَيَلزَمُ أَنَّ يُفْرَدُ القِسمُ الضَّعِيفُ فَيُقَالُ مَثلا الكَلِمَةُ قِسمان اسمٌ وَفِعل والقِسمُ الثَّالِثُ هُوَ الحَرفِ الرَّابِعُ أَنَّ تَعْيِيرَ الحُكمِ مِن الخُصُوصِ إِلَى العُمُومِ لَا يُمكِنُ إِلَّا بِتَقْرِيرِهِ فِي صُورَةٍ أُخرَى وَهُوَ مَعَنَى الأَصَالَةِ المُطلَقَةِ الخَامِسُ أَنَّ الإِجَمَاعَ أَيضًا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى السَّنَدِ فَيَنبَغِي أَن لَا يَكُونَ أَصَلا مُطَلَقًا وَالجَوَابُ عَن الأَوَّلِ أَنَّا لَا نَدْعِى أَنَّ لِعَدَمِ الفَرعِيَّةِ دَخَلًا فِي مَفهُومِ الأصلِ بَلْ إِنَّ الأصلَ مَقُولٌ بِالتَّشْكِيكِ وَإِنَّ الأصلَ الَّذِي يَستَقِلُّ فِي مَعنَى الْأَصَالَةِ وَابْتِنَاءِ الفرع عَلَيْهِ كَالكِتَابِ مَثَلًا أَقوَى مِن الأصلِ الَّذِي يَبْتَنِي فِي ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ بِحَيثُ يَكُونُ فَرعُهُ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ مُبتَنِيًّا عَلَى ذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ كَالقِيَاسِ وَالأضْعَفُ غَيْرُ دَاخِلٍ فِى الأصلِ المُطلَقِ بِمَعْنَى الكَامِلِ فِي الْأَصَالَةِ وَهَذَا بَيِّنٌ . وَأَمَّا الأبُ فَإِنَّمَا يَتَنِي عَلَى أَبِيهِ فِي الوُجُودِ لَا فِي الْأبُوَّةِ وَالأصَالَةِ لِلوَلَدِ فَلا يَكُونُ مِمَّا ذَكَرنَا فِي شَيْءٍ وَعَن الثَّانِي أَنَّ السَّبَبَ القَريب هُوَ المُؤكِّرُ فِى فَرعِهِ وَالمُفضِى إِلَيْهِ وَأَثَرُ البَعِيدِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِى الوَاسِطَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ السَّبَبُ القَرِيبُ لَا فِي فَرعِهِ فَبِالضَّرُورَةِ يَكُونُ أَولَى وَأَقوَى مِن البَعِيدِ فِي مَعْنَى السَّبَبِيَّةِ وَالْأَصَالَةِ لِذَلِكَ الفَرعِ وَفِيمَا نَحْنُ فِيهِ القِيَاسُ لَيسَ بِمُثبِتٍ لِحُكمِ الفَرعِ فَضَلا عَن أَن يَكُونَ قَرِيبًا لِيَكُونَ أُولَى بِالْأَصَالَةِ بَل هُوَ مُظهِرٌ لاستنَادِ حُكمِ الفَرعِ إِلَى النَّصَّ وَالإِجماع وَعَن الثَّالِثِ أَنَّا لَا نُسَلِّمُ لُزُومَ أَولَوِيَّةِ بَعضِ الأقسَامِ فِي كُلِّ تَقسِيمٍ وَكَيْفَ يُتَصَوَّرُ ذَلِكَ فِي تَقسِيمِ المَاهِيَّاتِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ إِلى أَنوَاعِهَا وَأَفَرَادِهَا كَتَقسِيمِ الحَيَوَانِ إِلَى الإِنسَانِ وَغَيرِهِ وَلَو سُلَّمَ لُزُومُ ذَلِكَ فِي كُلّ قِسمَةٍ فَلَا نُسَلِّمُ لُزُومَ الإِشَارَةِ إِلَى ذَلِكَ وَالتَّسْبِيهِ عَلَيْهِ غَايَةُ مَا فِي البَابِ أَنَّهُ يَجُوزُ وَعَن الرَّابِعِ أَنَّهُ إِن أُرِيدَ بِالتَّقْرِيرِ التَّقرِيرُ بِحَسَبِ الوَاقِعِ حَتَّى يَكُونَ القِيَاسُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُقَرِّرُ الحُكمَ وَيُشِتُهُ فِي صُورَةِ الفرع فَلا نُسَلّمُ امتِنَاعَ التَّغيير بدونِهِ وَإِن أُريدَ التَّقَرِيرُ بِحَسَبِ عِلمِنَا فَهُوَ لَا يَقتَضِي إِسْنَادَ الحُكمِ حَقِيقَةٌ إِلَى القِيَاسِ لِيَكُونَ أَصَلا لَهُ كَامِلا وَعَن الخَامِسِ بَعدَ تَسلِيم مَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّ الإِجْمَاعَ إِنَّمَا يَحتَاجُ إلَى السَّنَدِ فِي تَحَقُّقِهِ لَا فِي نَفْسِ الدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى الحُكمِ فَإِنَّ المُستَدِلَّ بِهِ لَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُلاحَظَةِ السَّنَدِ وَإِلا لِتِفَاتِ إِلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ القِيَاسِ فَإِنَّ الاستدلال بِهِ لَا يُمكِنُ بِدُونِ اعْتِبَارِ أَحَدِ الْأُصُولِ الثَّلَاثَةِ وَالعِلَّةِ المُستَبَطَةِ مِنهَا وَقَد يُجَابُ بِأَنَّ الإجمَاعَ يُثبتُ أَمْرًا زَايْدًا عَلَى مَا يُثبتُهُ السَّنَدُ وَهُوَ قطعِيَّةُ الحُكمِ بِخِلافِ القِيَاسِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُفِيدُ زِيَادَةً بَل رُبَّمَا يُورِثُهُ نُقصَانًا بأن يَكُونَ حُكمُ الأَصل قَطعِيًّا وَحُكمُهُ ظَنّى . ترجمہ:۔ اور اعتراض کئے گئے ہیں اس پر چند وجوہ کے ساتھ اول یہ کہ نہیں ہے کوئی معنی اصل مطلق کا مگر یہ کہ بنی ہو اس پر اس کا غیر برابر ہے کہ وہ (اصل) دوسری شئی کے لئے فرع ہو یا نہ ہو اس وجہ سے صحیح ہے اس اصل کا اطلاق کرنا اب پر اگر چہ وہ فرع ہے (جد کے اعتبار ہے ) الثانی شئی کا سبب قریب اولیٰ ہے باوجود یکہ وہ مسبب ہو بعید سے اسم سبب کا اطلاق کرنے کے ساتھ اس پر ( اولی ہے ) بعید سے اگر چہ نہ ہو ہو وہ مسبب دوسری شئی کے لئے الثالث بیشک بعض اقسام کا اولی ہو نا معنی مقسم میں یہ ہر تقسیم کو لا زم ہے پس لازم آئے گا کہ علیحدہ کیا جائے قسم ضعیف کو پس کہا جائے گا مثال کے طور پر کلمہ دوستم پر ہے اسم اور فعل اور قسم ثالث وہ حرف ہے۔ الرابع یہ کہ حکم کا تبدیل ہونا خصوص سے عموم کی طرف نہیں ہے ممکن مگر اس کے ثابت ہونے کے ساتھ دوسری صورت میں اور وہ اصول مطلقہ کے معنی میں ہے۔ الخامس بیشک اجماع بھی محتاج ہوتا ہے سند کی طرف پس مناسب یہ ہے کہ نہ ہو وہ (اجماع) اصل مطلق ۔ اور جواب اول اعتراض کا یہ ہے کہ بیشک ہم نہیں دعوی کرتے کہ بیشک فرع نہ ہونے کو کوئی دخل ہے اصل کے مفہوم میں بلکہ بیشک اصل کا اطلاق کیا گیا ہے تشکیک کے ساتھ اور بیشک وہ اصل جو مستقل ہوتا ہے اصالت کے معنی میں اور فرع کے معنی ہونے میں اس پر جیسا کہ کتاب مثال کے طور پر قوی ہے اس اصل سے جو معنی ہو اس
The second [objection] is that the proximate cause of a thing, even though it is caused by the remote cause, is more worthy of being called 'cause' than the remote cause, even if it is not caused by something else. The third is that the superiority of some divisions over others in the meaning of the thing being divided is necessary in every division, so it follows that the weak division must be singled out, e.g., 'the word has two divisions: noun and verb, and the third division is the particle.' The fourth is that changing a ruling from specific to general is not possible except by establishing it in another form, and that is the meaning of absolute originality. The fifth is that consensus also needs a basis, so it should not be considered an absolute principle. The answer to the first: we do not claim that not being a branch has any role in the definition of 'asl; rather, 'asl is said by analogy. The 'asl that is independent in the meaning of originality and on which a branch is based—like the Book, for example—is stronger than the 'asl that is based on something else in that meaning, such that its branch is in reality based on that other thing, like qiyas. The weaker is not included in the absolute 'asl in the sense of complete originality, and this is clear. As for the father, he is based on his father in existence, not in fatherhood and originality for the child, so it is not from what we mentioned. Regarding the second: the proximate cause affects its branch and leads to it; the effect of the remote cause is only on the intermediary, which is the proximate cause, not on its branch. So necessarily, it is more worthy and stronger than the remote cause in the meaning of causality and originality for that branch. In our case, qiyas does not establish the ruling of the branch, let alone be proximate to be more worthy of originality; rather, it reveals that the ruling of the branch is based on text or consensus. Regarding the third: we do not concede that the superiority of some divisions is necessary in every division. How can that be imagined in the division of real essences into their types and individuals, like the division of animal into human and non-human? Even if its necessity in every division is conceded, we do not concede the necessity of pointing to it and specifying it. The utmost in the matter is that it is permissible. Regarding the fourth: if by 'establishment' is meant establishment in reality such that qiyas establishes the ruling and places it in the branch, then we do not concede the impossibility of changing without it. If by 'establishment' is meant according to our knowledge, then it does not require attributing the ruling in reality to qiyas such that it becomes a complete principle for it. Regarding the fifth: after conceding what was mentioned, consensus needs a basis for its verification, not for the indication itself of the ruling. For the one who uses it as evidence does not need to consider its basis, otherwise he would need to rely on it, unlike qiyas, for using it as evidence is not possible without considering one of the three principles and the underlying cause derived from them. And it may be answered that consensus establishes something additional over what the basis establishes, namely the certainty of the ruling, unlike qiyas, for it does not provide any addition; rather, it may cause deficiency in that the original ruling may be certain and its ruling conjectural.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage presents five objections against the concept of 'asl mutlaq (absolute principle) and the Hanafi responses to them. The objections are: (1) If 'asl does not require being a branch (far'), then even a father (who is a branch of his own father) can be called an 'asl. (2) The proximate cause is more worthy of being called 'cause' than the remote cause, so qiyas, being a proximate method, should be the 'asl. (3) In a division, the stronger division should be specified, so if qiyas is the strongest, it should be called 'asl. (4) Changing a ruling from specific to general is only possible by qiyas establishing it in another form, making qiyas the absolute principle. (5) Ijma also needs a basis, so it cannot be an absolute principle. The author responds: (1) 'Asl is said with gradation; the independent 'asl (like the Quran) is stronger than qiyas which depends on another 'asl. The father is an 'asl in existence but not in originality for the child. (2) The proximate cause has direct effect; qiyas does not establish the ruling but only reveals its basis in text or consensus. (3) Not every division requires singling out a weak or strong part; e.g., the division of animal into human and non-human does not require specifying one. (4) If qiyas truly establishes the ruling, then it would be an 'asl, but we deny that; if it only clarifies our knowledge, it does not attribute the ruling to qiyas. (5) Ijma needs a basis for its existence but not for its evidentiary value; the mujtahid using ijma does not need to check its basis, unlike qiyas which always requires considering the original case and cause. Additionally, ijma adds certainty to the ruling, whereas qiyas may reduce certainty.

Key Terms
أصل مطلق (aṣl muṭlaq): An absolute principle that is independent and not based on another principle; the basis for a branch (far').
قياس (qiyās): Analogical deduction; deriving a ruling for a new case based on an original case (aṣl) due to a common ʿilla (effective cause).
تشكيك (tashkīk): Gradation; a term that applies to different instances with varying degrees of strength.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the fifth objection, why is Ijma considered an 'asl mutlaq despite needing a basis?
A) Because Ijma does not actually need a basis; it is a divine revelation.
B) Because Ijma requires a basis only for its verification, not for its evidentiary value, and it adds certainty to the ruling.
C) Because Ijma is based on the Quran and Sunnah, but so is qiyas.
D) Because Ijma is always unanimous.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is not an 'asl mutlaq because it is epistemologically dependent on an original source; only the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma are independent and complete in originality.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
12 min
Five Objections to Qiyas as a Deficient Root
Lists five logical objections to qiyas as deficient root with Hanafi responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five objections to qiyas as a deficient root and state the Hanafi responses to each.

Core Idea
Qiyas is called a deficient root because it depends on other sources, not because it is weak.

In Hanafi usul, qiyas (analogical reasoning) is considered a valid but dependent source, often called 'asl naqis' (deficient root). This classification has been contested with five logical objections. This lesson examines each objection and the Hanafi defense, clarifying why qiyas remains a subordinate source while still being essential for deriving rulings where no explicit text exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five objections
List each objection concisely from the passage: (1) Qiyas is an absolute root because it is a basis for rulings just like a father is a root for his son. (2) Qiyas is the proximate cause of rulings, so it deserves the title 'root' more than the remote three principles. (3) If weakness alone justifies separate mention, then every division should separate weaker types, but this is not done. (4) Qiyas changes specific rulings to general ones, and this change implies it affirms the ruling, making it a complete root. (5) Consensus also needs proof, so consistency would require calling consensus a deficient root as well.
2
Understand the Hanafi responses
For each objection, recall the response given in the commentary: (1) Being a basis does not make something an absolute root if it itself is a branch; a father is a root for his son but also a branch of his own father. (2) The proximate cause is indeed more worthy, but qiyas is not the proximate cause; it only manifests the ruling attributed to text or consensus. (3) The necessity of priority does not apply to all divisions, especially real essences; even if it did, indicating priority is permissible but not required. (4) If 'affirmation' means actual establishment, then others can establish rulings without qiyas; if it means according to scholars, it does not require attribution to qiyas. (5) Consensus needs proof only for verification, not for its own indication; qiyas needs constant reference to the three principles and the derived cause.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Note the type of argument used: The first objection relies on analogy (father-son); the second on causation (proximate vs. remote); the third on division and priority; the fourth on the function of change; the fifth on consistency with consensus. The Hanafi responses employ distinctions (e.g., between existence and principality, between verification and indication) and reject certain logical necessities.
4
Evaluate the strength of the defense
Consider whether the responses successfully rebut each objection. For example, the first response effectively clarifies that being a basis does not negate dependency. The second response emphasizes that qiyas is not truly proximate but only a manifestation. The third response denies the universality of the objection. The fourth response distinguishes between actual affirmation and scholarly affirmation. The fifth response highlights the unique need of qiyas for constant derivation.
5
Apply the framework to modern usul discussions
Recognize that similar objections arise today when critics argue that qiyas is either too weak or too strong. Understanding the Hanafi defense helps maintain methodological consistency: qiyas is a valid but subordinate source, and its rulings are zanni (speculative) compared to qat'i (definitive) rulings from primary sources.

الاول سے پہلا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ کا کہنا ہے کہ قیاس اصل ناقص ہے اصل کامل نہیں ہے کیونکہ پہلے تین اصول کی فرع ہے آپ کا یہ قول درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ اصل مطلق وہ ہوتا ہے جو غیر کے لئے مبنی علیہ بنے قطع نظر اس کے کہ وہ کسی کی فرع ہو یا نہ ہو جیسے باپ بیٹے کے لئے اصل مطلق ہے اگر چہ خود باپ اپنے باپ کی فرع ہے اب اس اعتبار سے قیاس احکام کے لئے مبنی علیہ ہے اس لئے قیاس کو اصل مطلق کہنا چاہئیے نہ کہ اصل ناقص ۔
الثانی: سے دوسرا اعتراض کہ اگر ایک چیز کے دو سبب ہوں ایک سبب قریب ہو اور دوسرا سبب بعید ہو تو اصل کا اطلاق سبب قریب پر زیادہ بہتر ہوتا ہے سبب بعید سے اگر چہ سبب قریب کسی چیز کی فرع ہو اور سبب بعید فرع نہ ہو پھر بھی سبب قریب پر اصل کا اطلاق زیادہ اولی ہوتا ہے جیسے تالا کھولنے کے دو سب ہیں سبب قریب حرکت مفتاح ہے اور سب بعید حرکت ید ہے اور سبب قریب یعنی حرکت مفتاح یہ سبب بعید یعنی حرکت ید کی فرع ہے اب عرف میں اصل کا اطلاق حرکت مفتاح میں اولی ہوتا ہے نہ کہ حرکت ید پر ایسے ہی حکم کو ثابت کرنے میں قیاس سبب قریب ہے اور اصول ثلثہ سبب بعید ہیں تو قیاس پر اصالت کا اطلاق اولی ہونا چاہئیے بنسبت اصول تختہ کئے۔
الثالث:۔ تیسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ نے کہا ہے کہ قیاس اصل ضعیف ہے اور باقی تینوں اصول وہ اصل کامل ہیں یعنی من کل الوجودہ احکام کو ثابت کرتے ہیں قیاس چونکہ اصل ضعیف ہے اس لئے اس کو علیحدہ ذکر کیا جاتا ہے اگر قیاس کو صرف اسی وجہ سے علیحدہ ذکر کیا جاتا ہے تو پھر ہر تقسیم میں غیر اولیٰ کو اولی سے علیحدہ ذکر کرنا چاہئیے کیونکہ ہر تقسیم میں بعض اقسام بعض سے اولیٰ ہوتی ہیں مثلا علم الخو میں کلمہ کی تین قسمیں ہیں ان میں سے اسم فعل قوی ہیں اور حرف ضعیف ہے تو کہنا چاہئیے کہ الکلمۃ علی قسمین اسم وفعل والقسم الثالث حرف حالا نکہ ایسے نہیں کہا جاتا۔
الرابع : چوتھا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ خود تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ قیاس خصوص کو عموم کی طرف تبدیل کر دیتا ہے مثلا شراب کی حرمت قرآن میں یہ خاص ہے شراب کے ساتھ لیکن قیاس نے بھنگ اور چرس میں بھی حرمت کا حکم لگا یا اب قیاس تغیر حکم کا فائدہ دے رہا ہے اور تبدیلی اس وقت تک نہیں ہو سکتی جب تک قیاس میں تقریر حکم ( حکم کو ثابت کرنا ) نہ پائی جائے مثلا قیاس جب تک افیون وغیرہ میں حکم ثابت اور مقرر نہیں کرے گا اس وقت تک عموم ثابت نہیں ہوگا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ عموم اس وقت ثابت ہو گا جب کہ حرمت ثابت ہو جب یہ حرمت ثابت ہوئی تو قیاس اصل کامل ہوا نہ کہ اصل ناقص ۔
الخامس: پانچواں اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ نے کہا قیاس اصل ناقص ہے کیونکہ یہ محتاج ہے اصول ثلثہ کا تو پھر اجماع بھی محتاج ہے سند کا تو اسے بھی اصل ناقص کہنا چاہئیے حالانکہ آپ اسے اصل کامل مانتے ہیں ۔
In meaning, another thing in the capacity that it is its branch, in reality based on that thing like qiyas, and the weaker does not enter into the absolute root included in the meaning of principality, and this is clear. And in any case now, it is nothing except that it is existent over its father in existence, not in being a father, and not in the meaning of principality for the child. So it is not among those which we mentioned in any thing. And the answer to the second objection is that indeed the proximate cause is effective in its branch and leads to it, and the effect of the remote cause is nothing except that it is effective in the intermediary which is the proximate cause, not in its branch. So necessarily the proximate will be more worthy and stronger than the remote cause, and in the meaning of principality for that branch, and that which we are discussing, that qiyas is not a establisher of the ruling of the branch, let alone that it be proximate so that it becomes more worthy with principality; rather it is a manifestation of the ruling being attributed to the text and consensus. And the answer to the third objection is that indeed we do not accept the necessity of priority for some types in every division, and how can it be conceived? In the division of real essences into their species and individuals, like the division of animal into human and other than it. And if one accepts its necessity in every division, then we do not accept the necessity of indication towards it and warning upon it. In this chapter, its limit is that it is permissible. And the answer to the fourth objection is that indeed if what is meant by "taqrir" is actual affirmation, so that qiyas is that which strengthens the ruling and establishes it in the form of a branch, then we do not accept the impossibility of others without it. And if what is meant is according to the scholars, then it does not require the ruling being attributed in reality to qiyas so that it becomes a complete root for it. And the answer to the fifth objection is that after accepting what has been mentioned, that indeed consensus is nothing except that it needs a proof in its verification, not in the very indication of the ruling, then indeed the one who argues by it will not need to consider the proof and turn to it, unlike qiyas. Then indeed the one who argues by it is not able to rely on any of the three principles without considering the cause that is derived from it. And sometimes it is answered in this way: that consensus establishes an additional matter beyond what the proof establishes, and its ruling is definitive, unlike qiyas; indeed it does not benefit that addition, and sometimes deficiency arises in it in such a way that the ruling of the original is definitive and its ruling is speculative. Objections to calling qiyas a deficient root. Commentary: "And it was objected by several aspects." This is the fifth part of the text; from here the commentator has quoted five objections. First objection: Your saying that qiyas is a deficient root, not a complete root, because it is a branch of the first three principles; this statement is not correct because the absolute root is that which serves as a basis for another regardless of whether it itself is a branch of something or not, like a father is an absolute root for his son even though the father himself is a branch of his own father. So from this perspective, qiyas is a basis for rulings, therefore qiyas should be called an absolute root, not a deficient root. Second objection: If one thing has two causes, a proximate cause and a remote cause, then applying the term "root" to the proximate cause is more appropriate than to the remote cause, even if the proximate cause is a branch of something and the remote cause is not a branch. Still, applying "root" to the proximate cause is more worthy. For example, opening a lock has two causes: the proximate cause is the movement of the key, and the remote cause is the movement of the hand. The proximate cause (movement of the key) is a branch of the remote cause (movement of the hand). But in common usage, applying "root" to the movement of the key is more worthy, not to the movement of the hand. Similarly, in establishing a ruling, qiyas is the proximate cause and the three principles are remote causes. So applying principality to qiyas should be more worthy compared to the three principles. Third objection: You have said that qiyas is a weak root and the other three principles are complete roots, i.e., they establish rulings from every aspect. Since qiyas is a weak root, it is mentioned separately. If qiyas is mentioned separately only for this reason, then in every division, the less worthy should be mentioned separately from the more worthy, because in every division some types are more worthy than others. For example, in grammar, there are three types of words: among them, nouns and verbs are strong, and particles are weak. So one should say: "The word is of two types: noun and verb, and the third type is particle." But this is not said. Fourth objection: You yourself admit that qiyas changes a specific ruling into a general one. For example, the prohibition of wine in the Qur'an is specific to wine, but qiyas applies the ruling of prohibition to bhang and charas as well. Now qiyas provides the benefit of changing the ruling, and change cannot occur unless qiyas contains affirmation (establishing the ruling). For example, qiyas will not establish general application until it establishes and affirms the ruling in opium etc. In summary, general application is established only when prohibition is established; when prohibition is established, then qiyas becomes a complete root, not a deficient root. Fifth objection: You have said that qiyas is a deficient root because it needs the three principles. Then consensus also needs proof, so it should also be called a deficient root, whereas you consider it a complete root.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is part of a commentary that lists five objections against the claim that qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a deficient root (asl naqis) as opposed to the three complete roots (Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma). The commentator (Sharh) presents each objection in detail, though the responses are summarized earlier. The first objection argues that qiyas is an absolute root because it serves as a basis for rulings, just as a father is a root for his son even though the father himself is a branch of his own father. The second objection uses the example of proximate and remote causes to argue that qiyas, being the proximate cause of rulings, deserves the title of root more than the remote three principles. The third objection points out that if weakness alone justifies separate mention, then every division should separate weaker types, which is not done (e.g., in grammar, particles are not mentioned separately). The fourth objection argues that qiyas changes specific rulings to general ones, and this change implies that qiyas affirms the ruling, making it a complete root. The fifth objection claims that consensus also needs proof, so consistency would require calling consensus a deficient root as well. The passage is important for understanding the Hanafi defense of qiyas as a valid source when properly conditioned.

Key Terms
اصل ناقص (asl naqis): A root that is deficient because it depends on other roots; specifically qiyas is considered deficient because it relies on Qur'an, Sunnah, or Ijma for its validity.
اصل کامل (asl kamil): A complete root that independently establishes rulings without needing another source; refers to Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma.
سبب قریب (sabab qareeb): The proximate cause that directly leads to an effect; in this context, qiyas is considered the proximate cause for deriving rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the fifth objection, why is qiyas considered deficient while consensus remains a complete root, even though both need proof?
A) Consensus needs proof only for verification, while qiyas needs constant reference to the three principles.
B) Consensus is based on multiple opinions, while qiyas is just one analogy.
C) Qiyas is weaker than consensus because consensus is always unanimous.
D) Qiyas was created later, so it is less authoritative.

Key Takeaway
The five objections to qiyas as a deficient root are refuted by the Hanafi school through precise distinctions, confirming qiyas' dependent and subordinate status without undermining its validity.

Objections to Qiyas as an Inferior Source and Responses
Presents five objections and responses to first three

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five objections against Qiyas as an inferior source and explain the Hanafi responses to the first three, particularly the concepts of homonymy of 'asl' and Qiyas as manifestor (muzhir) not establisher.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a defective source because 'asl' applies weakly to branches; it manifests rulings, not establishes them.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, Qiyas is considered a secondary source compared to the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'. This passage from a commentary on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih presents five hypothetical objections arguing that Qiyas should be a complete source, then provides the commentator's responses to the first three. Understanding these objections and responses deepens your grasp of the epistemological hierarchy of sources in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested classification
The central issue is whether Qiyas is a complete source (asl kamil) or a defective source (asl naqis). The objections argue for the former; the Hanafi position defends the latter.
2
Analyze the first objection and response
Study the objection's analogy of the father-son relationship. Then follow the Hanafi response that the term 'asl' is homonymous (mushtarak) and applies more strongly to independent sources.
Key term: qiyas ma' al-fariq (analogy with a difference).
3
Analyze the second objection and response
The objection uses the example of proximate vs. remote causes (key movement vs. hand movement). The response distinguishes between establishing a ruling (ithbat) and manifesting it (izhar); Qiyas is only a manifestor.
4
Analyze the third objection and response
The objection argues that if Qiyas is weak, it should be mentioned separately in every division. The response counters that real essences (mahiyat haqiqiyyah) do not have degrees of preferability; all individuals of a species are equal.
This objection uses an analogy from Arabic grammar (noun/verb/particle).
5
Connect to remaining objections
The fourth and fifth objections are not answered in this portion. Note that the fourth objection claims Qiyas changes rulings (takhsis to ta'mim) and the fifth argues that Ijma' also depends on proofs. Consider how the same principles might apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاول سے پہلا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ کا کہنا ہے کہ قیاس اصل ناقص ہے اصل کامل نہیں ہے کیونکہ پہلے تین اصول کی فرع ہے آپ کا یہ قول درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ اصل مطلق وہ ہوتا ہے جو غیر کے لئے مبنی علیہ بنے قطع نظر فرع نہ ہو پھر بھی سبب قریب پر اصل کا اطلاق زیادہ اولی ہوتا ہے جیسے تالا کھولنے کے دو سب ہیں سبب قریب حرکت مفتاح ہے اور سب بعید حرکت ید ہے اور سبب قریب یعنی حرکت مفتاح یہ سبب بعید یعنی حرکت ید کی فرع ہے اب عرف میں اصل کا اطلاق حرکت مفتاح میں اولی ہوتا ہے نہ کہ حرکت ید پر ایسے ہی حکم کو ثابت کرنے میں قیاس سبب قریب ہے اور اصول ثلثہ سبب بعید ہیں تو قیاس پر اصالت کا اطلاق اولی ہونا چاہئیے بنسبت اصول تختہ کئے۔ الثالث:۔ تیسرا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ نے کہا ہے کہ قیاس اصل ضعیف ہے اور باقی تینوں اصول وہ اصل کامل ہیں یعنی من کل الوجودہ احکام کو ثابت کرتے ہیں قیاس چونکہ اصل ضعیف ہے اس لئے اس کو علیحدہ ذکر کیا جاتا ہے اگر قیاس کو صرف اسی وجہ سے علیحدہ ذکر کیا جاتا ہے تو پھر ہر تقسیم میں غیر اولیٰ کو اولی سے علیحدہ ذکر کرنا چاہئیے کیونکہ ہر تقسیم میں بعض اقسام بعض سے اولیٰ ہوتی ہیں مثلا علم الخو میں کلمہ کی تین قسمیں ہیں ان میں سے اسم فعل قوی ہیں اور حرف ضعیف ہے تو کہنا چاہئیے کہ الکلمۃ علی قسمین اسم وفعل والقسم الثالث حرف حالا نکہ ایسے نہیں کہا جاتا۔ الرابع : چوتھا اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ خود تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ قیاس خصوص کو عموم کی طرف تبدیل کر دیتا ہے مثلا شراب کی حرمت قرآن میں یہ خاص ہے شراب کے ساتھ لیکن قیاس نے بھنگ اور چرس میں بھی حرمت کا حکم لگا یا اب قیاس تغیر حکم کا فائدہ دے رہا ہے اور تبدیلی اس وقت تک نہیں ہو سکتی جب تک قیاس میں تقریر حکم ( حکم کو ثابت کرنا ) نہ پائی جائے مثلا قیاس جب تک افیون وغیرہ میں حکم ثابت اور مقرر نہیں کرے گا اس وقت تک عموم ثابت نہیں ہوگا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ عموم اس وقت ثابت ہو گا جب کہ حرمت ثابت ہو جب یہ حرمت ثابت ہوئی تو قیاس اصل کامل ہوا نہ کہ اصل ناقص ۔ الخامس: پانچواں اعتراض یہ ہے کہ آپ نے کہا قیاس اصل ناقص ہے کیونکہ یہ محتاج ہے اصول ثلثہ کا تو پھر اجماع بھی اصل ناقص ہونا چاہیے کیونکہ یہ بھی سند کا محتاج ہوتا ہے اس لئے کہ اجماع خبر واحد پر منعقد ہوتا ہے تو اجماع محتاج ہوا سند و حدیث کا تو پھر اجماع کو بھی اصل ضعیف کہنا چاہئیے حالانکہ اجماع کو اصل کامل کہتے ہو۔ مذکورہ اعتراضات کے جوابات الجواب عن الاول :- یہ شرح کی عبارت کا چھٹا حصہ ہے یہاں سے شاریخ مذکورہ اعتراضات خمسہ کا جواب دے رہے ہیں ۔ پہلے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم نے یہ دعویٰ نہیں کیا کہ عدم فرعیت کو اصل مطلق ہونے میں دخل ہے کہ اصل ہوتا ہی وہی ہے جو کسی کی فرع نہ ہو بلکہ ہمارا دعویٰ یہ ہے کہ اصل کا لفظ کی [مشترک؟] ہے جس کا اطلاق بہت سارے افراد پر ہوتا ہے اور بعض افراد پر اطلاق اولی ہے اور دوسرے بعض افراد پر اصل کا اطلاق اضعف ہے یعنی اصل کا وہ فرد جو کسی کی فرع نہ ہو اس پر اصل کا اطلاق اقوئی ہے اور اصل کا اطلاق اس پر جو کسی کی فرع ہو ا ضعف ہے اس لئے اصل کا اطلاق قیاس پر اضعف ہے کیونکہ یہ فرع ہے اصول ثلثہ کی تو قیاس کو علیحدہ ذکر کرنے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے باقی رہا اس کو اب ، پر قیاس کرنا یہ قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے کیونکہ اب کی دو حیثیتیں ہیں (۱) تحقق ووجود فی الخارج کی حیثیت (۲) بیٹے کے لئے اصل ہونے کی حیثیت اور والد ہونے کی حیثیت اور پہلی حیثیت یعنی اپنے وجود و حق میں باپ اپنے باپ کی فرع ہے اور بیٹے کے لئے اصل ہونے کے اعتبار سے فرع نہیں ہے کیونکہ بیٹے کے لئے اصل ہونے میں یہ مستقل ہے اس میں ابو الاب کو کچھ دخل نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ بیٹے کے لئے اصل بنا ہے نکاح و جماع اور زوجہ کے ساتھ بخلاف قیاس کے کہ یہ اپنے تحقق و وجود میں اصول ثلثہ کی فرع نہیں ہے بلکہ احکام کے لئے اصل ہونے کی حیثیت سے فرع ہے اس لئے باپ پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔ عن الثانی:۔ دوسرے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بھائی ہم تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ سبب قریب اور اثر قریب پر اصل کا اطلاق اولیٰ ہے اس لئے یہ بلا واسطہ حکم کو ثابت کرتا ہے اور اثر بعید بالواسطہ حکم کو ثابت کرتا ہے لیکن قیاس تو مثبت حکم ہی نہیں ہے قیاس تو مظہر احکام ہے لہذا یہ اپنے حکم کے لئے سبب قریب نہیں ہو سکتا جب سبب قریب ہی نہیں ہے تو اصل مطلق کیسے بن سکے گا۔ عن الثالث : ۔ تیسرے اعتراض کے دو جواب دیئے ہیں ۔ جواب (1): ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ ہر تقسیم میں بعض افراد بغض سے اولیٰ ہوتے ہیں بلکہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی جو تقسیم اپنی انواع اور افراد کی طرف ہوتی ہیں ان میں سب افراد برابر ہیں مثلاً انسان کی تقسیم زید عمرو، بکر کی طرف ہے یہ سب انسانیت کے اعتبار سے برابر ہیں ایسے ہی حیوان کی تقسیم انسان ، بقر غنم کی طرف ہے یہ سب حیوان کے اعتبار سے برابر ہیں تو جہاں
First objection: You say Qiyas is a defective source, not a complete source because it is a branch of the first three sources. Your statement is not correct because the absolute source is that which is a basis for others regardless of whether it is a branch of something else. Like a father is an absolute source for his son even if the father himself is a branch of his own father. Accordingly, Qiyas is a basis for rulings, so Qiyas should be called an absolute source, not a defective source. Second objection: If a thing has two causes: a proximate cause and a remote cause, it is more appropriate to apply 'source' to the proximate cause than to the remote cause, even if the proximate cause is a branch of something else and the remote cause is not a branch. For example, opening a lock has two causes: the proximate cause is the movement of the key, and the remote cause is the movement of the hand. The movement of the key is a branch of the movement of the hand. Customarily, applying 'source' to the movement of the key is more appropriate than to the movement of the hand. Similarly, in establishing a ruling, Qiyas is the proximate cause and the three sources are the remote cause, so applying 'originating' to Qiyas should be more appropriate than to the three sources. Third objection: You said Qiyas is a weak source and the other three sources are complete sources, i.e., they establish rulings from all existence. Qiyas, being a weak source, is mentioned separately. If Qiyas is mentioned separately only for that reason, then in every division the less preferable should be mentioned separately from the more preferable, because in every division some parts are more preferable than others. For example, in grammar, the word is divided into three types: noun, verb, and particle. Among them, noun and verb are strong, and the particle is weak. Then it should be said: 'The word is of two types: noun and verb, and the third type is particle.' But it is not said like that. Fourth objection: You yourself admit that Qiyas changes the specific to the general. For example, the prohibition of wine in the Quran is specific to wine, but Qiyas also applies prohibition to bhang and cannabis. So Qiyas provides the benefit of changing the ruling, and change cannot occur unless Qiyas contains confirmation of the ruling (i.e., establishing the ruling). For example, until Qiyas establishes and confirms the ruling in opium etc., generality will not be established. Summary: Generality will be established only when prohibition is established. When this prohibition is established, then Qiyas is a complete source, not a defective source. Fifth objection: You said Qiyas is a defective source because it needs the three sources. Then Ijma' should also be a defective source because it too needs a proof, because Ijma' is established upon a solitary report. So Ijma' needs the proof of a hadith. Then Ijma' should also be called a weak source, whereas you call Ijma' a complete source. Responses to the mentioned objections: Response to the first: This is the sixth part of the commentary's text. Here the commentator responds to the five objections. The answer to the first objection is that we did not claim that not being a branch has a role in being an absolute source, such that a source is only that which is not a branch of something else. Rather, our claim is that the word 'asl' is homonymous (mushtarak) which applies to many individuals, and its application to some individuals is more appropriate, and to other individuals is weaker. That is, the individual of 'asl' that is not a branch of something else has a stronger application, and the application to that which is a branch is weaker. Therefore, the application of 'asl' to Qiyas is weaker because it is a branch of the three sources. So there is no harm in mentioning Qiyas separately. As for analogizing it to the father, that analogy is an analogy with a difference (qiyas ma' al-fariq) because the father has two aspects: (1) the aspect of existence and actuality in the external world, and (2) the aspect of being a source for the son and being a father. In the first aspect, the father, in his own existence, is a branch of his own father, but in the aspect of being a source for the son, he is not a branch because he is independent in being a source for the son; the grandfather has no role in that, because he is established as a source for the son through marriage and intercourse with the wife. Unlike Qiyas, which is not a branch in its own existence and actuality of the three sources, but is a branch in the aspect of being a source for rulings. Therefore, analogizing it to the father is an analogy with a difference. Regarding the second: The answer to the second objection is that we accept that the proximate cause and the proximate effect have a more appropriate application of 'source' because it directly establishes the ruling, and the remote effect establishes the ruling indirectly. But Qiyas is not the establisher of rulings; Qiyas is the manifestation of rulings. Therefore, it cannot be a proximate cause for its ruling. Since it is not a proximate cause, how can it become an absolute source? Regarding the third: The third objection has two answers: Answer (1): We do not accept that in every division some individuals are more preferable than others. Rather, in the division of real essences into their species and individuals, all individuals are equal. For example, the division of 'human' into Zayd, Amr, and Bakr: they are all equal with respect to humanity. Similarly, the division of 'animal' into human, cow, and sheep: they are all equal with respect to animality. So where...

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Tawdih wa Al-Talwih, presenting five objections against the classification of Qiyas as an inferior source (asl naqis) and then providing the commentator's responses to the first three objections. The objections argue that Qiyas should be considered a complete source (asl kamil) because it serves as a basis for rulings, just like a father is a basis for his son even though he himself is a son. The second objection uses the analogy of proximate vs. remote causes to argue that Qiyas should be considered the primary source. The third objection points out that if Qiyas were merely weak, it should be mentioned separately in all divisions, but that is not done. The fourth objection claims that Qiyas transforms specific rulings into general ones, implying it must first establish rulings, making it a complete source. The fifth objection argues that Ijma' also depends on proofs, so it should also be considered weak. In response, the commentator clarifies that the term 'asl' is homonymous; it applies more strongly to independent sources and more weakly to dependent ones. Qiyas is dependent on the three sources, so its application is weaker. The analogy to the father is invalid because a father's role as a source is independent in its own aspect, whereas Qiyas's role as a source is derivative. For the second objection, the commentator explains that Qiyas is not the direct establisher of rulings but only manifests them, so it cannot be a proximate cause. For the third objection, the commentator denies that real essences have degrees of preferability; all individuals of a species are equal, so the objection's analogical reasoning fails. Note that the responses to the fourth and fifth objections are not included in this portion of the text.

Key Terms
اصل ناقص (asl naqis): A source that is defective or incomplete because it depends on other sources for its authority; here applied to Qiyas.
اصل کامل (asl kamil): A complete source that does not depend on other sources for establishing rulings; here applied to the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma'.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings for new cases based on common illah with established cases; considered a defective source here.
مشترک (mushtarak): Homonymous; a word that has multiple meanings or applications. Here, 'asl' applies to both independent and dependent sources.
مظہر (muzhir): Manifestor; that which makes something apparent. In the response, Qiyas is described as a manifestor of rulings, not an establisher.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the response to the first objection, what is the key reason the commentator gives for why the analogy of a father (who is a source for his son despite being a son) does not apply to Qiyas?
A) The father is a source for his son in a different sense than Qiyas is a source for rulings.
B) The father's role as a source for his son is independent in its own aspect, while Qiyas's role as a source is derivative from the primary sources.
C) The father-son relationship is biological, not legal, so the analogy is irrelevant.
D) The commentator rejects the entire analogy as invalid without further explanation.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a defective source (asl naqis) because it depends on the primary sources for its authority; it does not establish rulings but only manifests them.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Refuting Objections to Qiyas as a Weak Asl
Commentator refutes five objections against qiyas being a weak asl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five objections against qiyas as a weak asl and evaluate the Hanafi responses using usul arguments.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a valid source but weaker than Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma due to its derivative nature.

In this passage, the commentator defends the Hanafi classification of qiyas as a weak asl (أصل ضعيف). Five objections had been raised, arguing that qiyas should be considered a strong independent source. The scholar systematically refutes each one, clarifying the nuanced meaning of 'asl' and the epistemological status of qiyas. Understanding this rebuttal is essential for grasping the Hanafi hierarchy of legal evidence and the proper use of analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Objections
List each objection as presented by the opponent. They concern: (1) the definition of asl, (2) proximate causality, (3) equality in divisions, (4) transformation of rulings, and (5) comparison with ijma'.
The commentator numbers them explicitly.
2
Extract the Hanafi Response for Each
For each objection, note the core rebuttal. The commentator distinguishes between strong and weak applications of 'asl', explains that qiyas is a manifestor not an establisher, argues that divisions may have unequal members, defines 'establishment' in reality vs knowledge, and distinguishes how ijma' differs from qiyas.
Focus on the logical move: qiyas ma'a al-fariq, permissibility of separate listing, etc.
3
Analyze the Use of Qiyas Ma'a al-Fariq
The commentator argues that analogizing qiyas to a father is an analogy with a disparity. Identify the two aspects of fatherhood and why they differ from qiyas.
This is a key rebuttal to the first objection.
4
Examine the Distinction Between Types of Need
In the fifth objection, the opponent claims ijma' also needs proof. The commentator splits 'need' into need for existence vs need for being an origin. Apply this to differentiate ijma' from qiyas.
This shows the depth of Hanafi usul reasoning.
5
Synthesize the Overall Position
After reviewing all five responses, state the Hanafi position: qiyas is an asl but weaker; it is subordinate to the three primary sources and is used only when no direct text or consensus exists.
Relate this to the practical hierarchy of evidence in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب عن الاول :- یہ شرح کی عبارت کا چھٹا حصہ ہے یہاں سے شاریخ مذکورہ اعتراضات خمسہ کا جواب دے رہے ہیں ۔ پہلے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم نے یہ دعویٰ نہیں کیا کہ عدم فرعیت کو اصل مطلق ہونے میں دخل ہے کہ اصل ہوتا ہی وہی ہے جو کسی کی فرع نہ ہو بلکہ ہمارا دعویٰ یہ ہے کہ اصل کا لفظ کی مشکلک ہے جس کا اطلاق بہت سارے افراد پر ہوتا ہے اور بعض افراد پر اطلاق اولی ہے اور دوسرے بعض افراد پر اصل کا اطلاق اضعف ہے یعنی اصل کا وہ فرد جو کسی کی فرع نہ ہو اس پر اصل کا اطلاق اقوئی ہے اور اصل کا اطلاق اس پر جو کسی کی فرع ہو ا ضعف ہے اس لئے اصل کا اطلاق قیاس پر اضعف ہے کیونکہ یہ فرع ہے اصول ثلثہ کی تو قیاس کو علیحدہ ذکر کرنے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے باقی رہا اس کو اب ، پر قیاس کرنا یہ قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے کیونکہ اب کی دو حیثیتیں ہیں (۱) تحقق ووجود فی الخارج کی حیثیت (۲) بیٹے کے لئے اصل ہونے کی حیثیت اور والد ہونے کی حیثیت اور پہلی حیثیت یعنی اپنے وجود و حق میں باپ اپنے باپ کی فرع ہے اور بیٹے کے لئے اصل ہونے کے اعتبار سے فرع نہیں ہے کیونکہ بیٹے کے لئے اصل ہونے میں یہ مستقل ہے اس میں ابو الاب کو کچھ دخل نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ بیٹے کے لئے اصل بنا ہے نکاح و جماع اور زوجہ کے ساتھ بخلاف قیاس کے کہ یہ اپنے تحقق و وجود میں اصول ثلثہ کی فرع نہیں ہے بلکہ احکام کے لئے اصل ہونے کی حیثیت سے فرع ہے اس لئے باپ پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔ عن الثانی:۔ دوسرے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بھائی ہم تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ سبب قریب اور اثر قریب پر اصل کا اطلاق اولیٰ ہے اس لئے یہ بلا واسطہ حکم کو ثابت کرتا ہے اور اثر بعید بالواسطہ حکم کو ثابت کرتا ہے لیکن قیاس تو مثبت حکم ہی نہیں ہے قیاس تو مظہر احکام ہے لہذا یہ اپنے حکم کے لئے سبب قریب نہیں ہو سکتا جب سبب قریب ہی نہیں ہے تو اصل مطلق کیسے بن سکے گا۔ عن الثالث : ۔ تیسرے اعتراض کے دو جواب دیئے ہیں ۔ جواب (1): ہم تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے کہ ہر تقسیم میں بعض افراد بغض سے اولیٰ ہوتے ہیں بلکہ ماہیات حقیقیہ کی جو تقسیم اپنی انواع اور افراد کی طرف ہوتی ہیں ان میں سب افراد برابر ہیں مثلاً انسان کی تقسیم زید عمرو، بکر کی طرف ہے یہ سب انسانیت کے اعتبار سے برابر ہیں ایسے ہی حیوان کی تقسیم انسان ، بقر غنم کی طرف ہے یہ سب حیوان کے اعتبار سے برابر ہیں تو جہاں برابر ہیں ان کو اکٹھے ذکر کر دیا جاتا ہے جیسے انسان کی تقسیم اس کے افراد کی طرف اور جہاں افراد برابر نہیں ان کو علیحدہ ذکر کر دیا جاتا ہے مثلاً القسم الرابع القیاس۔ جواب (۲):- اگر تسلیم کر لیا جائے کہ ہر قسم کے اقسام میں تفاوت ہوتا ہے بعض اقسام اولی ہوتے ہیں اور بعض اضعف تو پھر ہم یہ تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ اس کی طرف اشارہ اور تنبیہ ضروری اور واجب ہو کہ غیر اولی قسم کو علیحدہ ذکر کیا کریں زیادہ سے زیادہ ایسے کرنا جائز ہے تو اصولیوں نے جواز پر عمل کرتے ہوئے علیحدہ ذکر کر دیا اور نحویوں نے جواز پر عمل کرتے ہوئے اکٹھے ذکر کر دیا۔ وعن الرابع :۔ چوتھے اعتراض کا جواب یہ ہے کہ آپ نے کہا ہے کہ قیاس خصوص سے عموم کی طرف حکم کو تبدیل کرتا ہے اور تبد یلی بغیر تقریر فی الفرع کے نہیں ہو سکتی تو اس تقریر سے کیا مراد ہے؟ تقریر سے مراد تقریر فی نفس الامر ہے یا تقریر فی علمنا ہے اگر تو تقریر حکم فی نفس الامر مراد ہوتو پھر کوئی ضروری نہیں کہ اس تقریر کے بغیر تبدیلی نہ ہو کیونکہ مجتہد سے غلطی بھی ہو سکتی ہے اور اگر تقریر فی علمنا مراد لی تو لازم نہیں آتا کہ قیاس اصل مطلق و کامل ہو کیونکہ اصل مطلق تو تب ہو گا جب حکم کو نفس الامر میں ثابت کرے تو قیاس جب حکم کو نفس الامر اور حقیقت میں ثابت نہیں کر رہا تو اصل مطلق کا اطلاق قیاس پر درست نہیں ہوگا۔ عن الخامس بعد التسلیم :۔ میں پانچویں اعتراض کے پہلے جواب کی طرف اشارہ ہے پہلا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہمیں نہیں ہے کہ اجماع سند کا محتاج ہے ہو سکتا ہے کہ اجماع سند کا محتاج نہ ہو بلکہ اللہ تعالیٰ مسلمانوں کے ذہن میں ایک مسئلہ ڈال دیں اور اس مسئلہ پر اجماع منعقد ہو جائے اور صحابی کا قول بھی موجود نہ ہو تو یہ اجتماع بغیر سند کے ہوا اب یہ معتبر ہے اور اس میں احتیاج بھی نہیں ہے۔ جواب تسلیمی (۲): تعلیمی جواب یہ ہے کہ اگر ہم تسلیم کرلیں کہ اجماع سند کامحتاج ہوتا ہے تو احتیاج کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) اجماع اپنے تحقیق اور وجود فی الخارج میں سند کا محتاج ہو (۲) دوسرے احکام کے لئے اصل ہونے کے اعتبار سے محتاج ہو لیکن اجتماع میں پہلا احتیاج پایا جاتا ہے کہ یہ وجود اور تحقیق میں سند کا محتاج ہوتا ہے اور احکام کے لئے اصل ہونے کے اعتبار سے سند کا محتاج نہیں ہے یہی وجہ ہے کہ مستدل جب احکام ثابت کرتا ہے تو سند کی طرف اس کو احتیاجی نہیں ہوتی بخلاف قیاس کے کہ بغیر اصول ثلثہ کے تصور اور احتیاج کے مستدل قیاس سے احکام ثابت نہیں کر سکتا تو اس اعتبار سے اجماع اصل کامل ہے۔ جواب (۳) تعلیمی جواب یہ ہے کہ اجماع اگر چہ سند کا محتاج ہے لیکن اجماع ایک زائدہ بات کا فائدہ دیتا ہے کہ اجماع
Answer to the first: This is the sixth part of the commentary's expression. From here, the commentator responds to the five mentioned objections. The answer to the first objection is that we did not claim that non-derivativeness has a role in being an absolute original, such that only that which is not derived from something else is an origin. Rather, our claim is that the word 'asl' is ambiguous, applying to many individuals, and its application to some individuals is more appropriate, and to other individuals its application is weaker. That is, the individual of 'asl' that is not a derivative of anything else has a stronger application of 'asl', and the application of 'asl' to that which is a derivative of something else is weaker. Therefore, the application of 'asl' to qiyas is weaker because it is a derivative of the three principles. So there is no harm in mentioning qiyas separately. As for analogizing qiyas to a father, this is an analogy with a disparity because the father has two aspects: (1) the aspect of actual existence in the external world, and (2) the aspect of being an origin for the son and being a father. The first aspect, i.e., in his own existence and reality, the father is a derivative of his father, but in terms of being an origin for the son, he is not a derivative because in being an origin for the son, he is independent; the grandfather has no role in that, because the father became an origin for the son through marriage, intercourse, and wife. Unlike qiyas, which is not a derivative of the three principles in its own existence and realization, but is a derivative in terms of being an origin for rulings. Therefore, analogizing it to a father is an analogy with a disparity. Answer to the second: The answer to the second objection is that we accept that the application of 'asl' to the proximate cause and proximate effect is more appropriate because it establishes the ruling directly, while the remote effect establishes the ruling indirectly. But qiyas is not a establisher of rulings; qiyas is a manifestation of rulings. Hence, it cannot be a proximate cause for its ruling. When it is not a proximate cause, how can it become an absolute origin? Answer to the third: Two answers are given to the third objection. Answer (1): We do not accept that in every division some individuals are more appropriate than others. Rather, the division of real essences into their species and individuals—all individuals are equal. For example, the division of human into Zaid, Amr, and Bakr—these are all equal in terms of humanity. Similarly, the division of animal into human, cow, and sheep—these are all equal in terms of animality. Where they are equal, they are mentioned together, such as the division of human into its individuals. And where the individuals are not equal, they are mentioned separately, for example, the fourth division: qiyas. Answer (2): If it is accepted that in every type of division there is variation, some divisions being more appropriate and some weaker, then we do not accept that it is necessary and obligatory to point out and indicate that the less appropriate division should be mentioned separately. At most, doing so is permissible. The usul scholars, acting upon permissibility, mentioned it separately, and the grammarians, acting upon permissibility, mentioned it together. Answer to the fourth: The answer to the fourth objection is that you said that qiyas transfers a ruling from the particular to the general, and transformation cannot occur without establishment in the branch. What is meant by this establishment? Does it mean establishment in the reality itself or establishment in our knowledge? If it means establishment of the ruling in the reality itself, then there is no necessity that transformation cannot occur without this establishment, because a mujtahid can make a mistake. And if it means establishment in our knowledge, then it does not necessitate that qiyas is an absolute and complete origin, because an absolute origin would only be when it establishes the ruling in the reality itself. So when qiyas does not establish the ruling in the reality and truth, then the application of 'absolute origin' to qiyas is not correct. Answer to the fifth after acceptance: This refers to the first answer to the fifth objection. The first answer is that we do not concede that ijma' needs a proof. It is possible that ijma' does not need a proof; rather, Allah places a ruling in the minds of the Muslims and consensus occurs on that ruling, even if the saying of a Companion is not present. Thus this consensus occurred without proof and is valid, and there is no need. Acceptance answer (2): The educational answer is that if we accept that ijma' needs a proof, then need is of two types: (1) ijma' needs a proof in its own realization and existence in the external world; (2) it needs a proof in terms of being an origin for other rulings. But in ijma', the first need is found: it needs a proof in existence and realization, but in terms of being an origin for rulings, it does not need a proof. This is why when a deducer establishes rulings, it does not need a proof, unlike qiyas, because without the three principles and need, the deducer cannot establish rulings through qiyas. So in this respect, ijma' is a complete origin. Answer (3): The educational answer is that even if ijma' needs a proof, ijma' provides an additional benefit that it...
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rebuttal by the commentator of five objections raised against the classification of qiyas as a weak (ضعیف) source (اصل) in the Hanafi theory of legal sources. The objections likely came from opponents who argued that qiyas should be considered a strong source or that the classification is inconsistent. The commentator answers each objection systematically. 

First objection: The opponent argued that if an 'asl' is defined as something that is not derived from another, then qiyas, being derived from the three primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma), should not be called an 'asl' at all. The commentator clarifies that 'asl' is a polysemous term applied to many things with varying strength. The primary sources are 'asl' in the strongest sense because they are not derived from anything else; qiyas is 'asl' in a weaker sense because it is derived from them. Comparing qiyas to a father is rejected as an analogy with disparity (قیاس مع الفارق), since a father is an independent origin for his son, while qiyas is wholly dependent on the primary sources. 

Second objection: The opponent claimed that being a proximate cause makes an 'asl' absolute. The commentator responds that qiyas does not establish rulings (is not a cause) but merely manifests them (مظہر). Hence it cannot be an absolute 'asl'. 

Third objection: The opponent argued that in any division, some members are superior and should be listed separately. The commentator gives two answers: (1) In divisions of real essences (e.g., human types), all species are equal, so listing together is fine. Qiyas is a different category, not a species of the same genus. (2) Even if variation exists, mentioning the weaker separately is not obligatory but permissible; Hanafi usul scholars chose to mention qiyas separately for clarity, while grammarians chose to list together. 

Fourth objection: The opponent said qiyas transforms a ruling from particular to general, requiring establishment in the branch. The commentator questions the meaning of 'establishment': if in reality, transformation is possible even without establishment because mujtahids can err; if in knowledge, qiyas does not establish reality, so it cannot be an absolute 'asl'. 

Fifth objection: The opponent argued that ijma' also needs a proof, so why is it a complete 'asl' while qiyas is weak? The commentator gives three responses: (1) Ijma' can occur without a supporting text (Allah placing it in minds); (2) Even if ijma' needs proof for its existence, as an origin for other rulings it does not need proof, unlike qiyas; (3) Ijma' provides additional benefit of infallibility, strengthening its authority. 

Throughout, the commentator defends the Hanafi position that qiyas is a subordinate source, weak in itself, but valid for deriving rulings when no direct text exists.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): A source or origin; applied to the primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma) in a strong sense and to qiyas in a weaker sense due to its derivative nature.
فرع (far'): A derivative branch; qiyas is a far' of the three primary sources.
اجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Muslim community; considered a complete and independent asl, even if it may rely on a proof for its existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is qiyas not an absolute (mutlaq) asl?
A) Because qiyas is not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because qiyas does not establish rulings in reality (fi nafs al-amr).
C) Because qiyas is only used by the Hanafi school.
D) Because qiyas requires approval of the entire community.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that qiyas is a valid source (asl) but weaker than the three primary sources due to its derivative nature and lack of certainty in establishing rulings.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
6 min
Definition of Qa'idah and Its Syllogistic Application
Definition of qa'idah and its syllogistic application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qa'idah as a general usul principle and apply it using a syllogism to derive a ruling for a particular case from Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A qa'idah is a general rule that, via syllogism, yields rulings for all its particulars.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the term 'qa'idah' (plural: qawa'id) refers to a general legal principle that covers all its specific instances. This passage from the commentary of al-Taftazani explains how such a qa'idah is used to derive rulings for particular cases. The method is logical: you treat the particular as the subject of a minor premise and the qa'idah as the major premise, then conclude the ruling. This lesson unpacks that process and shows its application in both grammar and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of qa'idah
Read the first part of the passage where 'Allamah Taftazani defines qa'idah as 'a general ruling that encompasses all its particulars.' Note that this is broader than a single ruling; it is a universal principle.
2
Identify the two parts of the syllogism
The passage explains that to apply a qa'idah, you form a syllogism: the minor premise (sughra) is 'the particular is an instance of the qa'idah's subject' and the major premise (kubra) is the qa'idah itself. The conclusion is the ruling for the particular.
This mirrors Aristotelian logic: subject + predicate = conclusion.
3
Work through the grammatical example
Follow the example: qa'idah = 'Every fa'il is marfu'.' Particular: Zayd. Minor: 'Zayd is a fa'il.' Major: 'Every fa'il is marfu'.' Conclusion: 'Zayd is marfu'.'
This shows how a general rule from nahw (grammar) is applied.
4
Apply the same method to usul al-fiqh
The passage then gives a usul example: qa'idah = 'Every ruling proven by qiyas is established.' Particular ruling: 'This ruling is proven by qiyas.' Conclusion: 'This ruling is established.'
This illustrates how a qa'idah from usul al-fiqh works analogously.
5
Internalize the method for deriving fiqh rulings
Recognize that in Hanafi fiqh, qawa'id are used extensively. When faced with a new case, one must first find the relevant qa'idah, then check if the case falls under it, and finally derive the ruling via syllogism.
This is a core skill in usul al-fiqh: applying principles to particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقاعدة:۔ یہ عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے غرض اس سے یہ ہے کہ متن میں مذکور اصول فقہ کی تعریف میں قواعد کا لفظ تھا تو علامہ تفتازائی اس کی مرادی تحقیق بیان کرتے ہیں کہ قاعدہ ہر حکم کلی کو کہتے ہیں جو اپنی تمام جزئیات پر مشتمل ہو اور قواعد سے احکام مستقبط کرنے کا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ جس جزئی کا حکم معلوم کرنا ہے اس کو موضوع بنا دو اور قاعدہ کلیہ کے موضوع کو محمول بنا دو جب یہ موضوع و محمول ملیں گے تو صغری بن جائے گا اور قاعدہ کلیہ کو کبرٹی بنادیں گے پھر جو نتیجہ اخذ ہو گا وہ اس جزئی کا حکم ہوگا مثلاً نحویوں کا قاعدہ ہے کہ کل فاعل مرفوع اب ایک جزئی مثلاً زید کے بارہ میں معلوم کرتا ہے کہ اس پر کیا اعراب ہے تو اب زید کو موضوع بنا ئیں گے اور قاعدہ کلیہ کے موضوع یعنی فاعل کو معمول بنائیں گے تو زید فاعل یہ صغریٰ ہوا اور قاعدہ کلیہ کل فاعل مرفوع یہ کبرئی ہو احد اوسط کو خذف کر دیا تو زید مرفوع یہ نتیجہ ہوا یہ اس جزئی کا حکم ہے ایسے ہی مثال ہے اصول فقہ میں مثلاً اصول فقہ کی جزئی کو موضوع بنائیں گے اور قاعدہ کلیہ کے موضوع کو معمول بنا ئینگے مثلا ہذا الحکم دل القیاس علی ثبوتہ یہ صغریٰ ہے وکل حکم دل القیاس علی ثبوتہ فہو ثابت یہ کیرئی ہے اور نتیجہ ہذا الحکم ثابت یہ جزئی کا حکم ہے۔
And al-Qa'idah: This is the second part of the commentary's phrase. The purpose is that in the definition of Usul al-Fiqh mentioned in the matn, the word 'qawa'id' was used. So 'Allamah Taftazani explains its intended meaning: 'al-qa'idah' refers to every general ruling that encompasses all its particulars. The method of deriving rulings from the qawa'id is that you make the particular whose ruling you want to know the subject (mawdu'), and make the subject of the general rule the predicate (mahmul). When this subject and predicate meet, they become the minor premise (sughra), and the general rule becomes the major premise (kubra). Then the result obtained is the ruling of that particular. For example, the grammarians' rule is: 'Every subject (fa'il) is in the nominative case (marfu').' Now, if we want to know the case of a particular, e.g., Zayd, we make Zayd the subject, and we make the subject of the general rule, i.e., fa'il, the predicate, so we get 'Zayd is a fa'il' as the minor premise, and the general rule 'Every fa'il is marfu'' as the major premise. After omitting the middle term, we get 'Zayd is marfu'' as the conclusion – that is the ruling for this particular. Similarly, in Usul al-Fiqh, for example, we make a particular ruling of usul al-fiqh the subject, and the subject of the general rule the predicate, e.g., 'This ruling is proven by qiyas' is the minor premise, and 'Every ruling proven by qiyas is established' is the major premise, and the conclusion 'This ruling is established' is the ruling of that particular.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage defines 'qa'idah' as a general rule that covers all its particulars. The commentator then explains how such a rule is applied to derive rulings for specific cases, using a syllogistic method. In the example from grammar, the rule 'Every fa'il is marfu'' is applied to the particular 'Zayd' by forming a syllogism: Zayd is a fa'il (minor premise), every fa'il is marfu' (major premise), therefore Zayd is marfu' (conclusion). Similarly, in usul al-fiqh, a general rule about qiyas is applied to a specific ruling to conclude that it is established. This illustrates the process of deriving fiqh rulings from the general principles of usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
قاعدة (qa'idah): A general ruling that encompasses all its particulars; a principle from which rulings for specific cases are derived via syllogism.
صغرى (sughra): Minor premise in a syllogism; the statement that a particular case falls under a general category.
كبرى (kubra): Major premise in a syllogism; the general rule (qa'idah) itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first step in deriving a ruling for a particular case using a qa'idah?
A) Look for a direct Quranic verse.
B) Make the particular the subject of a minor premise.
C) Ask a mufti for a fatwa.
D) Compare the case to a similar one from the Prophet's time.

Key Takeaway
A qa'idah is a general ruling that, when applied syllogistically, yields the ruling for any particular case that falls under its subject.

The Definition and Function of Qa'idah in Usul al-Fiqh
Defining qa'idah and demonstrating its role in deductive reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qa'idah as a general ruling and apply it in a syllogism to derive a ruling for a particular case.

Core Idea
A qa'idah is a universal ruling that serves as the major premise in deductive reasoning for deriving specific fiqh rulings.

In this passage, Allama Taftazani clarifies the term 'qawa'id' (rules) from the definition of usul al-fiqh. He explains that a qa'idah is not just any statement but a general ruling (hukm kulli) that encompasses all its particulars. More importantly, he demonstrates how such rules are employed in a logical syllogism to derive rulings for individual cases. Understanding this concept is foundational for grasping how usul al-fiqh operates as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term and Its Definition
Locate the definition of qa'idah in the passage. Note that it is described as a general ruling (hukm kulli) that encompasses all its particulars (juz'iyyat).
The passage explicitly states: 'qa'idah refers to every general ruling that encompasses all its particulars.'
2
Recognize the Logical Structure
The passage explains a syllogism: the particular case becomes the subject (minor term), the subject of the general rule becomes the predicate, forming the minor premise (sughra). The general rule itself becomes the major premise (kubra).
This is a standard Aristotelian syllogism, adapted for legal derivation.
3
Follow the Grammatical Example
Work through the example: 'Every fa'il is marfu'' is the general rule. For a particular like Zayd, we establish 'Zayd is fa'il' as minor premise, then derive 'Zayd is marfu'' as conclusion.
This example illustrates the mechanism clearly before moving to fiqh.
4
Apply to an Usul al-Fiqh Example
The passage gives a usul example: minor premise 'This ruling is established by qiyas', major premise 'Every ruling established by qiyas is established', conclusion 'This ruling is established'. Recognize that the general principle (qa'idah) is the major premise.
This shows how usul al-fiqh rules function as major premises in derivations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقاعدة:۔ یہ عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے غرض اس سے یہ ہے کہ متن میں مذکور اصول فقہ کی تعریف میں قواعد کا لفظ تھا تو علامہ تفتازائی اس کی مرادی تحقیق بیان کرتے ہیں کہ قاعدہ ہر حکم کلی کو کہتے ہیں جو اپنی تمام جزئیات پر مشتمل ہو اور قواعد سے احکام مستقبط کرنے کا طریقہ یہ ہے کہ جس جزئی کا حکم معلوم کرنا ہے اس کو موضوع بنا دو اور قاعدہ کلیہ کے موضوع کو محمول بنا دو جب یہ موضوع و محمول ملیں گے تو صغری بن جائے گا اور قاعدہ کلیہ کو کبرٹی بنادیں گے پھر جو نتیجہ اخذ ہو گا وہ اس جزئی کا حکم ہوگا مثلاً نحویوں کا قاعدہ ہے کہ کل فاعل مرفوع اب ایک جزئی مثلاً زید کے بارہ میں معلوم کرتا ہے کہ اس پر کیا اعراب ہے تو اب زید کو موضوع بنا ئیں گے اور قاعدہ کلیہ کے موضوع یعنی فاعل کو معمول بنائیں گے تو زید فاعل یہ صغریٰ ہوا اور قاعدہ کلیہ کل فاعل مرفوع یہ کبرئی ہو احد اوسط کو خذف کر دیا تو زید مرفوع یہ نتیجہ ہوا یہ اس جزئی کا حکم ہے ایسے ہی مثال ہے اصول فقہ میں مثلاً اصول فقہ کی جزئی کو موضوع بنائیں گے اور قاعدہ کلیہ کے موضوع کو معمول بنا ئینگے مثلا ہذا الحکم دل القیاس علی ثبوتہ یہ صغریٰ ہے وکل حکم دل القیاس علی ثبوتہ فہو ثابت یہ کیرئی ہے اور نتیجہ ہذا الحکم ثابت یہ جزئی کا حکم ہے۔
And the rule: This is the second part of the phrase. The aim is that in the definition of usul al-fiqh mentioned in the matn, the word 'qawa'id' was used. So Allama Taftazani explains its intended meaning: 'qa'idah' refers to every general ruling that encompasses all its particulars. The method of deriving rulings from rules is: take the particular whose ruling is to be known as the subject, and take the subject of the general rule as the predicate. When these subject and predicate meet, they form a minor premise, and the general rule becomes the major premise. Then the conclusion derived is the ruling for that particular. For example, grammarians have a rule: 'Every subject (fa'il) is in the nominative case (marfu').' Now regarding a particular such as Zayd, we want to know its declension. So we make Zayd the subject and the subject of the general rule (i.e., fa'il) the predicate. Thus 'Zayd is a fa'il' becomes the minor premise, and the general rule 'Every fa'il is marfu'' becomes the major premise. Omitting the middle term, we conclude 'Zayd is marfu'' — this is the ruling for that particular. Similarly, in usul al-fiqh: we take a particular of usul al-fiqh as the subject and the subject of the general rule as the predicate. For example, 'This ruling is established by analogy (qiyas)' is the minor premise; 'Every ruling established by analogy is established' is the major premise; and the conclusion 'This ruling is established' is the ruling for that particular.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage clarifies the meaning of 'qawa'id' (rules) as used in the definition of usul al-fiqh. A qa'idah is a universal proposition (hukm kulli) that applies to all its instances (juz'iyyat). The process of deriving a ruling for a particular case from a general rule is a logical syllogism: the particular case is the minor term (subject, mawdu'), the general rule provides the major premise (kubra), and the conclusion is the ruling for that case. The commentator gives a clear example from Arabic grammar to illustrate this method, then applies it to a typical usul al-fiqh example. This shows how usul al-fiqh itself is a set of general rules from which specific fiqh rulings are derived through logical deduction.

Key Terms
قاعدہ (qa'idah): A general ruling (hukm kulli) that encompasses all its particulars (juz'iyyat).
جزئی (juz'i): A particular instance or case that falls under the general rule.
صغری (sughra): The minor premise in a syllogism, formed by linking the particular subject to the predicate of the general rule.
کبری (kubra): The major premise, which is the general rule itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A general ruling that encompasses all its particulars
C) A method of qiyas (analogy)
D) The minor premise in a syllogism

Key Takeaway
A qa'idah is a universal legal maxim that serves as the major premise in deductive reasoning for deriving specific rulings.

Qadiyya and Syllogism: The Logical Foundation of Usul al-Fiqh
Defining qadaya and syllogism for fiqh deduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a qiyas (sughra, kubra, awsat) and explain how a qa'ida kulliyya functions as the major premise in deducing fiqh rulings.

Core Idea
A fiqh ruling is derived by applying a universal qa'ida (kubra) to a specific case (sughra) via first-figure syllogism.

This passage from the Talwih commentary lays out the logical skeleton of Hanafi usul: how universal rules (qawaid kulliyya) serve as major premises in syllogisms that produce fiqh rulings. Understanding this architecture is essential for grasping how usul al-fiqh operates as a deductive science.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the multiple designations of a proposition
Note that a complete composite susceptible to truth/falsehood is called qadiyya, khabar, muqaddima, matlub, natija, mas'ala depending on perspective. The essence is one.
This shows the same entity can be viewed differently in different contexts.
2
Break down the syllogism structure
Identify the three terms: asghar (subject of conclusion), akbar (predicate of conclusion), and awsat (middle term repeated in both premises). Recognize the sughra (minor premise) and kubra (major premise).
The figures depend on the position of awsat.
3
Analyze the example: Hajj
Map the example: Hajj = asghar, wajib = akbar, ma'mur al-shari' = awsat. Sughra: 'Hajj is commanded by Lawgiver'. Kubra: 'Everything commanded by Lawgiver is obligatory'. Conclusion: 'Hajj is obligatory'. This is first figure.
4
Connect qa'ida kulliyya to kubra
Understand that the kubra in such derivations is a universal rule (qa'ida kulliyya) derived from the sources. The sughra is an easily obtainable fact about a particular case.
Thus usul al-fiqh is the study of these universal rules.
5
Grasp the scope of usul al-fiqh
The text states that acquiring the universal rule requires investigating the states of proofs, rulings, conditions, and restrictions. All those discussions fall under usul al-fiqh.
This defines the subject matter of usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
